
		<p>At a recent conference of international educators (AIEA &#8211; http://www.aieaworld.org/), representatives from VT, University of Basel and the Swiss Embassy in Washington DC served on a panel on the topic of global graduate education.  The focus of the discussion was on the Preparing the Future Professoriate Global Perspectives program and the U.S. &#8211; Swiss partnership.  After we described the program, the use of the term &#8220;global&#8221; was questioned due to the fact that our visits were limited to selected countries within continental Europe (e.g. France, Switzerland, Italy, Germany) or Chile.</p>
<p>The point raised by an audience member was a good one and prompted me to reflect on the term &#8220;global&#8221;.  While it is true the VT global perspectives program includes visits to selected universities and countries only, I would argue that the program provides a first hand experience that helps us to acknowledge differences around the world and embrace a perspective beyond our own country of origin.  The discussions in GRAD 5104 Preparing the Future Professoriate and brief presentations by international students provide a glimpse into higher education in various countries around the world.  The monthly seminars of the Global Perspectives group prior to departure provide the opportunity for more intentional focus on selected universities and countries.  Interactions among the participants enrich our understandings of each other in that not all of the VT participants are U.S citizens and not all University of Basel participants are Swiss.   We learn from each other about national perspectives, language and cultural differences and much more.  While &#8220;global&#8221; might signify the world, the experiences of the Global Perspectives program can plant the seeds and open our eyes and ears to greater understanding of our shared global world.</p>


		<p>Within the last two weeks, I have had the pleasure of interacting with multiple groupings of individuals who have or will participate in the Preparing the Future Professoriate: Global Perspectives program.  The initial meeting of the 2012 Global Perspectives group was held and we began the process of preparing for our May visit.  The PFP Chile &#8217;12 group met to debrief and reflect upon our January visit to Chile.  The weekly Global Perspectives lunch gathering was held and I was able to attend.  And most recently, I shared the draft itinerary with my colleagues who will participate in the first ever Global Perspectives program for graduate deans this July.  The pervasive sentiment across these groups is genuine excitement about learning more about the global academic world and reflecting about the significant impact upon one&#8217;s professional and personal lives.  The global perspectives program is one of the many ways through which we can and will transform graduate education.</p>


		<p>Graduate education has long focused on depth in the disciplines.  Academic disciplines have provided a system for organizing scholarship through academically accepted set of methodologies, professional journals and professional societies.  Disciplines tend to have a critical mass of scholars and educate students in the cultural norms of the discipline.  They have served and will continue to serve higher education well but more is needed.</p>
<p>With strong disciplinary foundations, we must now move and actively embrace and engage more in interdisciplinary graduate education.  Due in part to the inherent complexity of nature and society, very interesting and challenging research questions can be found at the interface between and among disciplines &#8211; these will require an interdisciplinary perspective.  This  leads scholars and graduate students to explore problems and research questions that are not confined to a single discipline and require collaborative endeavors. National agencies (e.g. <a href="http://www.nsf.gov/">NSF</a>,<a href="http://www.nih.gov/"> NIH</a>) have called for more interdisciplinary efforts and provided funding opportunities such as the Integrative Graduate Education and Research Training (<a href="http://www.igert.org/">IGERT</a>) programs.</p>
<p>This brings me to the topic of depth or breadth and beyond.  Arguments are frequently put forward that graduate education should focus on depth of knowledge and thus, we must maintain a disciplinary focus and focus on the disciplines. Along with this argument, it is often acknowledged that although interdisciplinary graduate education does provide valued breadth, it lacks the depth.  I&#8217;ve worked with interdisciplinary graduate education for the past 20 years and would argue that interdisciplinary research not only requires but can achieve both depth and breadth as well as integration for success.</p>
<p>In my recent musings about interdisciplinary graduate education and research, I have come to visualize those with the depth of training and education in the discipline as I-educated individuals, those with the depth and breadth of training and education plus the ability to work across disciplinary lines at T-educated individuals, and those individuals with breadth and depth of education and training in more than one discipline and the ability to integrate knowledge across disciplines as Pi (π)-educated individuals. Through this lens, knowledge seems to be grounded in and emanate from the disciplines and then integrated across boundaries.  These are the scholars and scientists who will be able to serve as the adaptive innovators of and for the 21st century.</p>
<p>.<a href="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2012/03/images1.jpg"><img data-attachment-id="73" data-permalink="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/kpdtge/index.php/2012/03/28/interdisciplinary-thinking-pi-and-adaptive-innovators/images-2/" data-orig-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2012/03/images1.jpg" data-orig-size="225,225" data-comments-opened="1" data-image-meta="{&quot;aperture&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;credit&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;camera&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;caption&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;created_timestamp&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;copyright&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;focal_length&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;iso&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;shutter_speed&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;title&quot;:&quot;&quot;}" data-image-title="images" data-image-description="" data-medium-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2012/03/images1.jpg" data-large-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2012/03/images1.jpg" class="alignnone  wp-image-73" src="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2012/03/images1-150x150.jpg" alt="" width="60" height="60" srcset="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2012/03/images1-150x150.jpg 150w , https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2012/03/images1.jpg 225w " sizes="(max-width: 60px) 100vw, 60px" /></a><strong></strong><strong></strong></p>

<div>“Universities, therefore, will have to reconsider the priorities and practices of graduate education and training in order to prepare individuals……. We argue that graduate programs must not only educate future scientists to be experts in the methods, techniques, and knowledge of their chosen disciplines but to have the broader problem-solving skills that require learning, unlearning, and relearning across disciplines.”</div>
<div></div>
<div><em>Rhoten, D. 2004. Interdisciplinary Research: Trend Transition. Items and </em><em>Issues 5, no. (1-2):6-11. </em></div>

		<p>Two great universities &#8211; the <a title="Sorbonne" href="http://www.english.paris-sorbonne.fr">Sorbonne</a> (Paris &#8211; Sorbonne University) and the <a href="http://www.unistra.fr/index.php?id=homepage">University</a> of Strasbourg.  Each has a long history and rich traditions.   Visiting each of these recently only reinforced their place in history of higher education, especially French higher education. They have stood the test of time.  And yet over the years, they have changed and there is more change to come.  I learned during the recent visit that the universities in Paris including the University of Pierre and Marie Curie (UPMC) will be engaged in merger and transformation &#8211; quite a significant change I imagine.  We will watch with great interest.   <a href="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2012/03/IMG_4493-Version-2.jpg"><img data-attachment-id="33" data-permalink="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/kpdtge/index.php/2012/03/12/sorbonne-and-strasbourg/img_4493-version-2/" data-orig-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2012/03/IMG_4493-Version-2.jpg" data-orig-size="1893,2096" data-comments-opened="1" data-image-meta="{&quot;aperture&quot;:&quot;4.5&quot;,&quot;credit&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;camera&quot;:&quot;Canon PowerShot G10&quot;,&quot;caption&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;created_timestamp&quot;:&quot;1331039945&quot;,&quot;copyright&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;focal_length&quot;:&quot;24.978&quot;,&quot;iso&quot;:&quot;200&quot;,&quot;shutter_speed&quot;:&quot;0.02&quot;,&quot;title&quot;:&quot;&quot;}" data-image-title="IMG_4493 &#8211; Version 2" data-image-description="" data-medium-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2012/03/IMG_4493-Version-2-270x300.jpg" data-large-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2012/03/IMG_4493-Version-2-924x1024.jpg" class="alignnone size-medium wp-image-33" src="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2012/03/IMG_4493-Version-2-270x300.jpg" alt="" width="270" height="300" srcset="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2012/03/IMG_4493-Version-2-270x300.jpg 270w , https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2012/03/IMG_4493-Version-2-924x1024.jpg 924w " sizes="(max-width: 270px) 100vw, 270px" /></a></p>
<p>These two universities will play an important role in the visits of the Global Perspectives programs organized for this summer.  In May 2012, the Future Professoriate Global Perspectives program participants will visit the University of Strasbourg as part of our visit to universities of the Upper Rhine and partners of the University of Basel.  While in Strasbourg, we will visit the campus and learn first-hand about the the University, the Doctoral Schools, and the French system of higher education.</p>

program designed for graduate deans from U.S. universities will visit the Sorbonne (the UPMC and other universities in Paris) as well as the University of Strasbourg.  It will be quite wonderful to experience the visit with two different groups (graduate deans, graduate students) and to watch as the streets of Paris reveal the universities hidden in plain sight and as the tram meanders the streets of Strasbourg and onto the central campus of the University of Strasbourg.</p>


		<p>The 2012 <a href="http://www.orpheus-med.org/" target="_blank">Orpheus</a> (<strong>OR</strong>ganisation for <strong>Ph</strong>D Education in Biomedicine and Health Sciences in the <strong>EU</strong>ropean<strong> S</strong>ystem) <a title="orpheus conference" href="http://http://www.orpheus2012.org/en/" target="_blank">conference</a> was held April 19-21 in <a href="http://visitbergen.com/en/" target="_blank">Bergen</a>, Norway.  I was invited to provide a plenary keynote entitled Global Doctoral Education: Critical Conversations on Saturday.  And thus, my journey began to learn more about Orpheus the organization, the individual members of the organization and to be able to listen to their conversations about evaluation of the PhD in the biomedicine and health science in Europe.</p>
<p>Nearly 200 individuals from many countries around Europe attending &#8211; almost all of them from the Biomedicine and Health Sciences disciplines.  A few of them from the European University Association (EUA) Council on Doctoral Education (CDE) and European Commission and several from the private sector.  Doctoral students and post docs were also in attendance and had a significant input into the collective wisdom to be captured in the consensus documents produced at the end of the conference.  It was pointed out at the open session that I was the only U.S. citizen in attendance &#8211; an actually delightful experience because the focus was on Europe and I was afforded the opportunity to hear their issues, concerns, and recommendations.</p>
<p>One &#8220;hot&#8221; topic was the implementation of <a title="Standards document" href="http://www.orpheus-med.org/images/stories/documents/ORPHEUS-AMSE-WFME-standards-for-PhD-education.pdf" target="_blank">Standards for PhD Education in Biomedicine and Health Sciences in Europe</a>.  A plenary session and multiple workshops were held about the use of these standards and of course, the perspectives differed.  Some wanted standards to be externally evaluated, others thought standards were fine but should include internal evaluation (and external), and some of us thought that the &#8220;standards&#8221; should be used more as guidelines for improvement of PhD evaluation. The final consensus has not yet been published and we await the result.  I found the discussion of terminology such as standards, guidelines, and accreditation most interesting as well as the discussion of the relationship between an external evaluating body and the use of evaluation in funding doctoral students.  A fascinating example of different cultural differences and the European system at work.</p>
<p>After only a few hours of listening, I was struck by two topics that kept coming up:  significance of the <a href="http://http://www.eua.be/eua/jsp/en/upload/Salzburg_Report_final.1129817011146.pdf">Salzburg principles</a> (part of the Bologna process documents) and the support for interdisciplinary (and multidisciplinary) research.  There were many times, it appeared to me that the speakers were referencing multi but I appreciated the acknowledgement that interdisciplinary research and graduate education were important.  (This would be a theme of my presentation on Saturday).  There was even some mention of &#8220;training&#8221; of PhD students, pedagogy and the links between education and research although most of the conversations focused on research in a more traditional sense.  Given this perspective, I knew that my presentation would take a different approach and would challenge the audience to think about higher education, examine the PhD as it currently exist and encourage critical reflections about the &#8220;conversations&#8221; in which graduate schools must engage.  The critical conversations I proposed included interdisciplinarity, teaching/learning in the academy, communicating science, innovation, social media, access to and within higher education, and collaboration.  You are familiar with these topics due to our conversations in the Future Professoriate program, especially the Global Perspectives program.</p>
<p>Bergen is a world heritage city and well worth a visit.  Given its location in the Norway, the days were long already (dark at 10pm), and the weather mild (no rain for 2 days which is highly unusual for Bergen).  I enjoyed the gathering of colleagues from the biomedical fields across the European continent and know that the conversations will continue.</p>


		<p>In a recent article entitled <a href="http://www.nafsa.org/about/default.aspx?id=30005">&#8220;Global citizenship: What are we talking about and why does it matter?&#8221;</a>, Madeleine F. Green offered her reflections about global citizenship.   The electronic version of the article traveling rapidly throughout the international education communities and beyond.  There are many within community and outside international education that are wrestling with terms such as internationalization, global education and global citizenship.  The conversation has been ongoing for many years so that topic is not new but embracing a global perspective seems to be ever more critical in today&#8217;s world.</p>
<p>For many, you know that I would definitely be seen as an advocate for enabling individuals to become global citizens.  Obviously this would be most apparent in the Graduate School&#8217;s commitment to global higher education through the <a href="http:/http://graduateschool.vt.edu/graduate_school/tge/index.html">Transformative Graduate Education</a> initiative and more specifically through the <a href="http:/http://www.vtnews.vt.edu/articles/2010/09/092710-outreach-globalperspectives.html">future professoriate global perspectives</a> program.  The Global Perspectives <a href="http:/https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/globalperspectives/">blog</a> stands as a visible representation of the growing interest in &#8220;understanding global&#8221;.</p>
<p>I hope that most of the broader university community would be in agreement with Green&#8217;s statement that the world would be a better place if colleges and universities could &#8220;produce graduates with knowledge and the disposition to be global citizens&#8221;.  From my perspective, &#8220;producing graduates&#8221; would apply not just to the undergraduate experience but to graduate students as an integral component of their preparation as the future leaders, scientists, scholars, artists, and educators.  Further, it is my firm belief that university administrators should engage and encourage the faculty and staff at our colleges and universities to become more engaged with a global understanding of the world.  Although the most frequent example of global engagement is study abroad, not everyone can have this experience.  And therefore, establishing global understanding among all of the university constituencies would require that global awareness and understanding come not only from experiences abroad but also from authentic global experiences &#8220;at home&#8221;.   This would be a challenge but should be among the goals of a 21st century university.</p>
<p>In the quest toward &#8220;producing&#8221; global citizens, colleges and universities will need to envision and develop programs and opportunities (abroad and at home) that are meaningful and relevant, incorporate active participation and individual engagement, and designed intentionally toward reaching the goal of global understanding.  These programs and opportunities are critical but perhaps most important is the realization that the journey towards becoming a global citizen must begin with introspection, a greater understanding of one&#8217;s own cultural context(s) and how these influence every day life to inform ourselves as global citizens.</p>
<p>The VT Future Professoriate Program stands as an example through which the study abroad (global perspectives program) and study-at-home (GRAD 5104) portions help prepare future faculty as global citizens.  The partnership with UniBasel provides a similar experience starting in Basel and then a visit to selected universities in the US.  This year, participants from the University of Lund will join us at <a href="http://http://www.oired.vt.edu/cesa/index.htm">CESA</a> in Riva San Vitale for a global graduate education seminar.  Although as participants in the 2012 Future Professoriate global perspectives program our individual journeys began from our unique place, the journeys have begun to converge and become enriched by the shared space and common experiences on the path to greater global understanding and becoming an informed citizen of our global society.</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>


		<p>An early train from Capolago-Riva San Vitale through the Swiss Alps to Zurich.  Mostly quiet enjoying the views and lost in my thoughts about the 2012 Global Perspectives program.  A special experience which none of us wanted to end.</p>
<p>It was great to share time with colleagues from UniBasel and Lund at CESA exploring access and higher education.  The conversations will continue and not just in Blacksburg and at the Swiss Embassy.   Let&#8217;s continue the dialogue and make global higher education our shared reality. Thanks to all!</p>


		<p>A week ago today, we gathered in Zurich to begin our journey.  Since then, we have visited 7 universities varying in mission and size; one more to go.  We met with Presidents (Rectors) of three universities (University of Basel, University of Strasbourg, and University of Lugano).  We heard many presentations about the academic programs, toured labs and facilities, talked with students and administrators.  We traveled by train mostly, a few buses and walked for many kilometers on a daily basis.  We have enjoyed finely prepared meals paired with outstanding wines.  We have visited cultural sites and explored the local scenery and customs.</p>
<p>Last Wednesday, we arrived in <a href="http://http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Riva_San_Vitale">Riva San Vitale </a>&#8211; our home for the next week.  The VT Faculty Development program participants were already in residence in Riva.  Our itineraries overlapped and thus were able to share travels and conversations.</p>
<p>Today is a holiday in Switzerland and the Santa Croce Church will open tomorrow for the first time in 2+ years.  We will visit the opening and witness a celebration of cultural heritage.  Another rich experience among many of the trip.  Still more to come.</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<p><a href="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2012/05/Riva_San_Vitale_3423_a3.jpg"><img data-attachment-id="259" data-permalink="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/kpdtge/index.php/2012/05/27/week-one-complete/riva_san_vitale_3423_a-4/" data-orig-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2012/05/Riva_San_Vitale_3423_a3.jpg" data-orig-size="" data-comments-opened="1" data-image-meta="[]" data-image-title="Riva_San_Vitale_3423_a" data-image-description="" data-medium-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2012/05/Riva_San_Vitale_3423_a3.jpg" data-large-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2012/05/Riva_San_Vitale_3423_a3.jpg" class="alignnone size-full wp-image-259" src="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2012/05/Riva_San_Vitale_3423_a3.jpg" alt="" /></a></p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>


		<p>On May 8th, the last of our monthly meetings of the 2012 VT Future Professoriate: Global Perspectives program was held prior to our individual departures to Switzerland.  Although our departure dates vary, the official start of our collective experience begins at 3pm on May 20th in the lobby of Hotel St. Josef in Zurich Switzerland.</p>
<p>A couple of days later, I ran into<a href="http://archdesign.vt.edu/faculty/lucy-ferrari"> Lucy Ferrari</a> (co founder of<a href="http://www.oired.vt.edu/cesa/index.htm"> CESA</a>) in President Steger&#8217;s office on the eve of her return home to Switzerland.  She expressed excitement and pleasure that yet another Global Perspectives group would join her for dinner at her favorite restaurant overlooking Lugano and Italy &#8211; <a href="http://www.carona-tourism.ch/de/gastronomie/ristorante-san-grato.html">Ristorante San Grato</a>.</p>
<p>As the days to departure decrease, the ability to focus on the global perspectives trip increases.  Commencement(s) are done.  The Honorable Michelle Obama and Senator Mark Warner spoke at VT commencement.  The speeches were inspiring and intensity (security) great.  The students are on their way &#8211; some are traveling in Italy, some in Switzerland and others to countries beyond.  I&#8217;ll be the last to fly &#8211; and then we will meet in Zurich.</p>
<p>During the previous Global Perspectives programs (this is the 7th year), I have encouraged participants to &#8220;look&#8221; and &#8220;see&#8221; the world around them.  I have especially encouraged the observations of doors and windows which I find provide wonderful views into the culture of the places we visit.  Yes, the &#8220;doors and windows&#8221; are obvious cultural markers and can easily serve as an initial focus for viewing the world.  And yet, observations should extend well beyond simply the doors and windows as physical objects to &#8220;doors and windows&#8221; as metaphor for &#8220;seeing&#8221; to understand and appreciate the culture.  During our individual and collective global perspective experience, our lives can be enriched by opening the doors and the views provided through the windows.  Let the journey begin.</p>


		<p>The theme of the 2012 Global Graduate Education Summit is &#8220;access to and within higher education&#8221;.  [The summit is a culminating event for Virginia Tech&#8217;s Future Professoriate Global Perspectives program held in Riva San Vitale, Switzerland in partnership with University of Basel in Switzerland and University of Lund, Sweden].  What follows is a preview of my musings in preparation for the opening presentation of the summit on May 29, 2012.</p>
<p>&#8220;Access to&#8221; and &#8220;access within&#8221; means many different things and we will explore the various meanings and global trends during the Summit.  At least initially, &#8220;access to&#8221; seems to make reference those who do, those who do not and those who should be able to seek admission into a college or university.  Historically specific groupings of individuals have not been able to access higher education but there seems to be emphasis on increasing access to colleges and universities in various parts of the world.  And with the advances in technology and the digital evolution the question must be raised about access to colleges and universities in a more traditional sense of higher education vs access to higher education in the sense of advanced knowledge and understanding not necessarily housed within colleges and universities.</p>
<p>&#8220;Access within&#8221; refers to those within the academy and how they access knowledge and understanding through teaching and learning, research/scholarship and engagement within higher education.  How information is taught.  How knowledge is acquired.  How learners learn. How one accesses information within and across disciplines or not.  How research questions are developed and shared or not.  The ways in which learning, discovery and engagement are or could be connected. These are just some of the topics that will be explored in the Summit.</p>
<p>&#8220;Access&#8221; also refers to access to the scholarship and information discovered (or uncovered) within the university.  Questions here could include who has access to the scholarship currently and how the public could access the information.  Open Access? Scholarship as private good or public good?  Education as private good or public good?  And there&#8217;s more.</p>
<p>Issues of access are intriguing and complicated &#8211; exciting conversations to follow.</p>


		<p>On July 22, 2012 the Tour de Academe <a href="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/gpgraddeans/">(Graduate Deans&#8217; Global Perspectives Program</a>) begins in Paris just as the <a href="http://www.letour.fr/us/index.html">Tour de France</a> ends.  The cyclists will ride along the Champs-Élysées to finish <a href="http://www.letour.fr/2012/TDF/COURSE/us/2000/etape_par_etape.html">Stage 20</a>.  Shortly after the Tour de France awards ceremony near the <a href="http://www.arcdetriompheparis.com/">Arc de Triomphe</a>, we will join  our hosts for the reception and dinner on the 24th floor of the University of Pierre and Marie Curie (<a href="http://www.upmc.fr/en/index.html">UPMC</a>) with a striking view of Paris including the Arc de Triomphe, the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eiffel_Tower">Eiffel Tower</a> (not shown in this photo), the Louvre, and much more.</p>
<p><a href="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2012/06/IMG_3828.jpg"><img data-attachment-id="289" data-permalink="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/kpdtge/index.php/2012/06/23/tour-de-academe-tour-de-france/img_3828/" data-orig-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2012/06/IMG_3828.jpg" data-orig-size="2592,1944" data-comments-opened="1" data-image-meta="{&quot;aperture&quot;:&quot;4.5&quot;,&quot;credit&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;camera&quot;:&quot;Canon PowerShot G10&quot;,&quot;caption&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;created_timestamp&quot;:&quot;1309961195&quot;,&quot;copyright&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;focal_length&quot;:&quot;30.5&quot;,&quot;iso&quot;:&quot;200&quot;,&quot;shutter_speed&quot;:&quot;0.004&quot;,&quot;title&quot;:&quot;&quot;}" data-image-title="IMG_3828" data-image-description="" data-medium-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2012/06/IMG_3828-300x225.jpg" data-large-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2012/06/IMG_3828-1024x768.jpg" class="alignleft size-thumbnail wp-image-289" src="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2012/06/IMG_3828-150x150.jpg" alt="" width="150" height="150" /></a></p>
<p>The 2012 Global Perspectives Program &#8211; Graduate Deans begins at 3pm (1500) at Hotel Maxim in the Latin Quarter, Paris with our first meeting of the <a href="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/gpgraddeans/who-are-we/">graduate deans</a> who have chosen to participate in this experience.  This program is a natural extension of the Global Perspectives Program offered through Virginia Tech Graduate School and through the University of Basel.  For more information, please visit the GPP graduate deans<a href="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/gpgraddeans/"> blog</a>.</p>


		<p>Lukas said it.  Justin tweeted it.  We all felt it (perhaps also the audience at the Swiss Embassy).  The 2012 Global Perspectives Program (GPP) has come to &#8220;the end but the global perspectives process continues&#8221;.</p>
<p>The global seminar on &#8220;access to and within higher education&#8221; at the <a title="Swiss Embassy in Washington DC" href="http://www.eda.admin.ch/washington">Embassy of Switzerland</a> on June 15, 2012 officially ended the GPP&#8217;12 program.  The well attended event featured a keynote presentation by <a href="http://chronicle.com/article/Karin-Fischer-Bio/48567/">Karin Fischer</a>, Senior Reporter for the Chronicle of Higher Education on the topic of access to higher education.  The GPP&#8217;12 participants from Virginia Tech, University of Basel and the University of Lund shared their reflections on access to and within through panel presentations and discussion with the audience.  Key <a href="https://twitter.com/?utm_campaign=kpdepauw&amp;utm_content=213447207561596928&amp;utm_medium=fb&amp;utm_source=fb#!/GPPVT">highlights</a> of conference are available via twitter.</p>
<p>During my drive back to Blacksburg, I took time to reflect upon GPP&#8217;12 and take note of the many and varied aspects of the program, the interactions with each of the participants, and the ways in which the program has once again changed our lives.  As seen through my lens, the program was indeed very successful! Thanks to all who participated, organized and contributed to the program&#8217;s success (<a href="http://internationalaffairs.unibas.ch/joint-programmes/joint-visiting-programmes/gpp/">UniBasel</a>, Embassy of Switzerland, <a href="http://www.lunduniversity.lu.se/">University of Lund</a>, <a title="VT Graduate School" href="http://graduateschool.vt.edu/">VT</a>).  I am grateful to each person because I know that one&#8217;s active engagement with the Global Perspectives program truly is making a difference and contributing to the overall success of the program.   And of course, to the process of preparing global citizens through global conversations.  Keep up the good work and let the conversations continue!</p>
<p>In July, I will lead a group of U.S. Graduate Deans on a similar <a title="Global Perspectives x 4" href="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/kpdtge/index.php/2012/02/05/global-perspectives-x-4/">global experience.</a>  Thus, the program expands and the process continues.</p>
<p>For more information about the Global Perspectives program, please follow <a href="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/pfpswitzerland">this blog</a>, the <a href="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/globalperspectives/">Global Perspectives</a> blog, <a href="https://twitter.com/?utm_campaign=kpdepauw&amp;utm_content=213447207561596928&amp;utm_medium=fb&amp;utm_source=fb#!/GPPVT">twitter</a>, and Face Book &#8211;<a href="https://www.facebook.com/pages/Global-Perspectives-Programme-University-of-Basel/119816501460078">GPP UniBasel</a>.</p>


		<p>The<a href="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/gpgraddeans/"> Global Perspectives Graduate Deans</a> program ended on Friday in Lugano and I traveled back to Zurich for one day prior to my return to the U.S.</p>
<p>As I walked around Zurich today, I could hear the voices of GPP alumni, I could see your faces in the crowds, and I was reminded of moments of sipping cappucino, eating bratwurst, strolling along the lake, watching the swans, buying chocolate, putting luggage on the train, walking the stairs to ETH and University of Zurich, and much much more.  Although I did stop &#8220;counting people&#8221; when the program ended, your presence was still alive in Zurich.</p>
<p>There are many stories spanning the seven years of the Global Perspectives Program including multiple programs (Switzerland, Chile, GradDeans).  These programs cut across universities, countries, languages, and more.  And they hold very fond memories indeed.  It has been my great pleasure to share these personal and professional experiences with the global perspectives program (GPPers) as well as my graduate dean colleagues. Thank you for joining me on these adventures toward greater global understanding and helping to enrich <a href="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/globalperspectives/">global perspectives</a>.  Read, share and contribute to the blogs.  Follow us on twitter @GPPVT and @kpdepauw (and more).  Find us on Facebook <a href="https://www.facebook.com/pages/Virginia-Tech-Graduate-School/348802603577#">(VT Graduate School</a>, <a href="https://www.facebook.com/pages/Global-Perspectives-Programme-University-of-Basel/119816501460078">UniBasel GPP</a>, <a href="https://www.facebook.com/GlobalPerspectivesProgramVirginiaTech">GPPVT</a>).</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>


		<p>In a recent <a title="Tour de Academe – Individual pathways to a shared journey" href="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/kpdtge/index.php/2012/07/20/tour-de-academe-individual-pathways-to-a-shared-journey/">blog</a>, I wrote about our individual pathways and the <a title="Tour de Academe – Individual pathways to a shared journey" href="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/kpdtge/index.php/2012/07/20/tour-de-academe-individual-pathways-to-a-shared-journey/">Tour de Academe.</a>  On Sunday, July 22nd our individual pathways converged in Paris and our collective journey started.  And now, the week-long Graduate Deans Global Perspectives program ’12 (<a href="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/gpgraddeans/">GPGradDeans</a>) has come to an end.  Our last meeting was a joint gathering with <a href="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/pfpswitzerland/">GPP Switzerland</a> alumni from Virginia Tech and <a href="http://internationalaffairs.unibas.ch/joint-programmes/joint-visiting-programmes/gpp/">University of Basel</a> at VT’s Center for European Studies and Architecture (<a href="http://www.oired.vt.edu/cesa/index.htm">CESA</a>). Rich discussions were held and insights gathered.</p>
<p>During the program, the Graduate Deans visited University of Pierre and Marie Curie (<a href="http://www.upmc.fr/en/index.html">UPMC</a>) and the historic <a href="http://www.english.paris-sorbonne.fr/the-university/">Sorbonne University</a>, the<a href="http://www.unistra.fr/index.php?id=homepage"> University of Strasbourg</a>, the <a href="http://www.unibas.ch/index.cfm?o_lang_id=2">University of Basel</a>, and the <a href="http://www.uzh.ch/index_en.html">University of Zurich</a>. We discussed graduate education and the future of higher education with Presidents (Rectors) from three universities. We learned about their versions of graduate schools (e.g., doctoral college, <a href="http://www.grc.uzh.ch/index_en.html">graduate campus</a>). We met with administrators and faculty.  And our program began with dialogue with colleagues from the European University Association (<a href="http://www.eua.be/Home.aspx">EUA</a>).  For details of our visit, see <a href="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/gpgraddeans/index.php/itinerary/">itinerary</a> and read associated links.</p>
<p>This visit was nestled between the end of the Tour de France In Paris and the Opening Ceremonies of the 2012 Olympic Games. The weather was perfect throughout the week (Paris, Strasbourg, Basel, Zurich, Lugano) &#8211; a little hot at times but virtually no rain.  Our visits required the use of three languages beyond English: French, German (more precisely Swiss German) and Italian. The cuisine spanned countries and cultures. We traveled by plane, bus, tram, metro, train, and quite often by foot &#8211; sometimes up hill and sometimes with luggage. Lakes, mountains, rivers, plains, and terraced hills (e.g., vines) surrounded us daily.  A collective journey of shared experiences and individual reflections&#8230;.we have taken photos, blogged, and tweeted.  We had readers following our blogs and &#8220;followers&#8221; on twitter (@kpdepauw, @GPPVT and more).  We had families traveling with us who shared in our experiences on a daily basis.</p>
<p>The Global Perspectives program has touched many lives across many universities, many countries and across many years.  As a result, I believe that the Graduate Deans and GPP alumni now share in the responsibility to expand the reach and enrich the global experiences and perspectives of others.  It has been my great pleasure to share personal and professional experiences with my graduate dean colleagues.  Thank you for joining me on this adventure toward greater global understanding and enhanced global graduate education.</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>


		<p>While driving to the Roanoke airport this morning to catch my flight to Dulles and then Paris, I knew that our <a href="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/kpdtge/index.php/2012/06/23/tour-de-academe-tour-de-france/">Tour de Academe</a> had finally begun.  But in reality, the journey actually began some months ago as the Global Perspectives &#8211; Graduate Deans program was conceptualized, the itinerary developed and individual decisions were made to participate.  Although titled GP Graduate Deans, the individuals in this shared journey include graduate deans (of course), the organizers and hosts of our Tour de Academe visits, and the Global Perspectives Program (GPP) alumni from VT and UniBasel.</p>
<p>The individual pathways were set.  Some would bring family.  Some would travel solo.  Collectively, we would travel by car, bus, train, metro and airplane and arrive from many different places.  By now, some have already left their home place.  Many are traveling today as I am.  And some are preparing for departure in a few days to arrive for the Graduate Deans/GPP alumni event in Riva San Vitale in the Italian speaking part of Switzerland.</p>
<p>Today I could almost sense and did visualize the movement of the individuals toward our shared journey.  Different places, different times, and different pathways leading to the shared experience.  Each of us will leave our homes and work places and travel toward a common place called Paris &#8211; the Latin Quarter and the Hotel called Maxim to start our shared journey on Sunday, July 22, 2012.  This portion is the Graduate Deans journey and we will be joined by colleagues from the EUA and universities in Paris, Strasbourg, Basel, and Zurich.  They too are a part of the shared global perspective experience in that we can learn from each other as we engage to understand global higher education.  And along the way, we meet and interact with those we know and those we can only observe in the spaces in which we interact (streets of Paris, trains, restaurants and more).  We observe the culture, the cuisine, the building (windows and doors), Swiss Alps, towns, cities, lakes, and more.  These too are important components of the global experience.  The shared journey will culminate at VT&#8217;s Center for European Studies and Architecture (CESA) and will be enriched through the reflections and interactions of GPP alumni from VT and UniBasel who will travel along their unique pathways to reach our shared space.</p>
<p>The place and time are set &#8211; the program is in motion.  We await the arrival of the participants who will come and go throughout the journey.  Although this program will last but a week, but the journey toward global perspectives will continue.  As Lukas said of the <a href="http://newadventuresinhied.wordpress.com/2012/06/27/gpp-2012-the-basel-experience/">2012 GPP</a> Basel experience, the program ends but the process continues.</p>


		<p>Our flights are booked.  Hotel rooms reserved.  Restaurants prepared to share their cuisine.  Our academic and university hosts are eager to welcome us.  And of course, the Virginia Tech Center for European Studies and Architecture (CESA) is prepared for the arrival of the graduate deans and the GPP alumni.  After hundreds of emails, numerous meetings, many phone calls and lots of discussions across nearly 9 months, the plans have been finalized for the Graduate Deans Global Experience (July 22 -27) and the GPP alumni reunion (July 26-27).  Enthusiasm appears high and we are ready.  Follow our experiences through our blogs (<a href="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/globalperspectives/">Global Perspectives</a>, <a href="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/gpgraddeans/">Graduate Deans</a>, or individual blogs) and follow us on twitter (@kpdepauw).</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>


		<p>Twice in as many weeks, an incoming graduate student asked me (and other admnistrators) what Virginia Tech&#8217;s tagline &#8220;invent the future&#8221; meant to us.  While &#8220;invent the future&#8221; can and does have multiple meanings and various interpretations, the video by Michael Marantz entitled &#8220;<a href="http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=rDiUVS_-4_Q">the future is ours</a>&#8221; visually represents what &#8220;invent the future&#8221; means to me.</p>
<p>Michael Marantz created the video to inspire and it does.  I share Marantz&#8217;s view that the future is exciting and holds &#8220;immense&#8221; possibilities.  He challenges us to commit to possibilities and work hard to achieve them.  Further, I believe that his message has particular relevance for graduate education today, especially at a university with a tagline of &#8220;invent the future&#8221;.  For graduate students are indeed the future scholars, teachers, leaders, artists, entrepreneurs and more.  Their innovations and imaginations will continue well into the 21st century. They will help solve the grand challenges facing a global society and they will serve society (in keeping with VT&#8217;s motto <em>ut prosim</em>).  Graduate students independently and often in collaboration with others (faculty, staff and undergraduates) can and will &#8220;invent the future&#8221;.</p>
<p>&#8220;Invent the future&#8221; has been VT&#8217;s tagline for many years now and has helped to frame the university&#8217;s agenda.  Invent the future served recently as the foundation upon which the work of the Task Force on Instructional Technology: VT 2020 was designed.  In its <a href="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/inventthefuture2020/">report</a>, the Task Force articulated its vision of the possible and next steps for technology at Virginia Tech.  The <a href="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/inventthefuture2020/">blog</a> format allows for readers to engage with its critical reflections and commentary, informative narratives and perspectives, and of course links to timely videos and relevant materials available on the internet.  This report and others provided  the initial backdrop to the planning process for the university.  In its commitment to a &#8220;progressive agenda&#8221;, Virginia Tech recently prepared <a href="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/vtstrategic/">A Plan for a New Horizon</a> Envisioning Virginia Tech 2012-2018.  The path forward to inventing the future is articulated in the plan and incorporates growth and expansion of graduate education at VT among its foci.  Graduate education is a critical component for the future of Virginia Tech.</p>
<p>Graduate education is about discovering and advancing knowledge and preparing for life and work as citizens in a globally diverse world. Graduate education is about innovation and creativity; it is about exploring possibilities and embracing change.  It is about knowing that the <a href="http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=rDiUVS_-4_Q">future is ours</a>, truly.</p>


		<p>In higher education, we are taught to find meaning and to make sense of &#8220;our world&#8221;.  We work with data (although described differently by different disciplines).  We analyze and synthesize information.  We look for patterns &#8211; we look for trends.  We reconcile differences.  We identify the obvious and even sometimes we are encouraged to look beyond the immediate and the apparent to that which is unobvious.</p>
<p style="text-align: left">Images and visuals are a part of our everyday lives.  Sometimes, they are ubiquitous; so much so that we might no longer see the obvious let alone the unobvious.  One visual that comes to mind is the FedEx logo.  The FedEx logo has become a relatively common sight.  It appears on packages, trucks, offices, and websites.  It comes in different sizes, shapes and colors.  And so you ask, why am I writing about FedEx?  (This is not a product endorsement but it does provide a good example).   Brief background.</p>
<p style="text-align: left"><img src="http://www.logodesignlove.com/images/negative/fedex-logo-design.jpg" alt="FedEx logo design" width="147" height="72" /></p>
<p style="text-align: left">I heard Daniel Pink, bestselling author of <a href="http://www.danpink.com/books/whole-new-mind"><em>A Whole New Mind</em></a> and <a href="http://www.danpink.com/books/drive"><em>Drive</em>, </a>speak at an annual meeting of the Council of Graduate Schools and would shortly thereafter invite him to VT as the 2006 Distinguished Graduate School Speaker.   Pink argued convincingly that we have entered the &#8220;conceptual age&#8221; in which the &#8220;whole mind&#8221; is needed to excel in today&#8217;s society.  His more recent work shares some &#8220;<a href="http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=u6XAPnuFjJc">surprising</a>&#8221; insights into what truly motivates us.</p>
<p style="text-align: left">There&#8217;s much to learn from Pink&#8217;s work but a simple story about his son&#8217;s observation gives opportunity for reflection about the importance of that which we might not be able to see.  In his presentations, Daniel Pink often shares a story about his son viewing the FedEx truck and describing it as &#8220;the truck with the arrow on it&#8221;.  In this case, a child&#8217;s view reveals that which is obvious to him but not necessarily so obvious to others.</p>
<p style="text-align: left"><img src="http://blog.fedex.designcdt.com/sites/all/themes/fedex/logo.png" alt="FedEx Home" width="134" height="51" /></p>
<p style="text-align: left">It is easy to see the obvious (the words FedEx) but until encouraged to do so, we might not see the &#8220;arrow&#8221; (space between the E and the x).   As readers, we often tend to see and focus on the words and attend less so on the visual representation that appears in the <a href="http://xfinity.comcast.net/slideshow/finance-hiddenlogomeanings/fedex/">&#8220;white&#8221; space</a>.  There are many other examples but two are included here.  In the examples below, one can definitely see what is meant to be obvious but the unobvious &#8211; the Bronx skyline and the outline of the Australian continent &#8211; requires greater attention to the visuals.</p>
<p><a href="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2013/02/the-bronx-zoo-logo-large-e1360523358817.jpg"><img data-attachment-id="503" data-permalink="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/kpdtge/index.php/2012/10/24/seeing-the-unobvious-fed-ex-and-other-visuals/the-bronx-zoo-logo-large/" data-orig-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2013/02/the-bronx-zoo-logo-large-e1360523358817.jpg" data-orig-size="300,616" data-comments-opened="1" data-image-meta="{&quot;aperture&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;credit&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;camera&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;caption&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;created_timestamp&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;copyright&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;focal_length&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;iso&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;shutter_speed&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;title&quot;:&quot;&quot;}" data-image-title="the-bronx-zoo-logo-large" data-image-description="" data-medium-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2013/02/the-bronx-zoo-logo-large-145x300.jpg" data-large-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2013/02/the-bronx-zoo-logo-large-e1360523358817.jpg" class="alignleft size-thumbnail wp-image-503" src="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2013/02/the-bronx-zoo-logo-large-150x150.jpg" alt="" width="150" height="150" /></a></p>
<p><img src="http://www.logodesignlove.com/images/negative/yoga-australia-logo.jpg" alt="Yoga Australia logo design" width="238" height="151" /></p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<p>Our challenge is to have the unobvious become as real as the obvious.</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>


		<p>For the fourth time this year, I has the wonderful opportunity to visit UniBasel regarding the Global Perspectives program.  The &#8220;Input Seminar&#8221; as the beginning of the UniBasel GPP&#8217;12, the VTGPP &#8217;12 visit to UniBasel, the Graduate Deans Global Perspectives visit in July and the November GPP &#8217;12 Alumni event.   It feels so comfortable being in Switzerland and Basel.</p>
<p>The Alumni event focused on <a href="http://www.internationalaffairs.unibas.ch/gpp">Careers paths in academia: How well do we select?</a>  In attendance were GPP participants from all three years of the UniBasel program.  What a delight to see the alumni and to catch up with their lives &#8211; degrees finished, post doc positions, new jobs in academia and graduate studies in progress.  The visit enforced the desire to keep in touch with all Global Perspectives program participants &#8211; VT and UniBasel.  Stay tuned for the LinkedIn alumni site to be developed soon.</p>
<p>The Global Perspectives Program has a way of changing lives.  We meet  new people from different countries and cultures.  We explore topics facing global higher education.  We learn about our own higher education systems.  We share our thoughts in writing and through presentations and ongoing dialogue.  We learn more about ourselves and our ability to help make change in the world.  It was a privilege to watch the alumni offer their reflections on &#8220;access to and within higher education&#8221;, diversity and the purpose of the university.  Thanks for sharing and thanks for being a part of GPP!  It was a good trip &#8220;back to Basel&#8221;.</p>

our memories.  Thank you.</p>
<p>Please keep posting updates on your global adventures!</p>


		<p><a href="http://www.michigandaily.com/content/james-duderstadt-former-president-who-reshaping-higher-education">James Duderstadt</a>, President Emeritus of University of Michigan, is reshaping higher education.  He has written and spoken about a university as a social institution with social responsibility as described in his book entitled <em>A University for the 21st Century </em>published by the University of Michigan Press in 2000.</p>
<p><img data-attachment-id="471" data-permalink="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/kpdtge/index.php/2013/01/13/communicating-scholarly-endeavors/4117nz5x0hl-_bo2204203200_pisitb-sticker-arrow-clicktopright35-76_aa300_sh20_ou01_-2/" data-orig-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2013/01/4117NZ5X0HL._BO2204203200_PIsitb-sticker-arrow-clickTopRight35-76_AA300_SH20_OU01_1-e1358114888831.jpg" data-orig-size="165,217" data-comments-opened="1" data-image-meta="{&quot;aperture&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;credit&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;camera&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;caption&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;created_timestamp&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;copyright&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;focal_length&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;iso&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;shutter_speed&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;title&quot;:&quot;&quot;}" data-image-title="4117NZ5X0HL._BO2,204,203,200_PIsitb-sticker-arrow-click,TopRight,35,-76_AA300_SH20_OU01_" data-image-description="" data-medium-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2013/01/4117NZ5X0HL._BO2204203200_PIsitb-sticker-arrow-clickTopRight35-76_AA300_SH20_OU01_1-e1358114888831.jpg" data-large-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2013/01/4117NZ5X0HL._BO2204203200_PIsitb-sticker-arrow-clickTopRight35-76_AA300_SH20_OU01_1-e1358114888831.jpg" class="alignnone size-thumbnail wp-image-471" style="color: inherit;font: normal normal normal 15px/normal 'Helvetica Neue', Helvetica, Arial, sans-serif;font-style: inherit;font-weight: inherit;line-height: 1.625;border-style: solid;border-color: #dddddd;cursor: default;margin-top: 0.4em;height: auto;border-width: 1px;padding: 6px" src="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2013/01/4117NZ5X0HL._BO2204203200_PIsitb-sticker-arrow-clickTopRight35-76_AA300_SH20_OU01_1-e1358114961401-111x150.jpg" alt="" width="111" height="150" /></p>
<p>Social responsibility is especially important for a land grant university like Virginia Tech. One of the ways in which a univerity can exercise its social responsibility is through sharing the research and scholarly endeavors of the faculty and the students, especially graduate students.  The &#8220;open access&#8221; movement is a possibility for sharing university&#8217;s research but I&#8217;ll save that discussion for another blog.  Instead, I wish to reflect about the ways in which a research land grant university like Virginia Tech can meet its responsibility to share the results of research.</p>
<p>As faculty and graduate students we have historically been educated and trained to communicate our research/scholarship to those within our discipline primarily.  We learn how to prepare powerpoint or keynote presentations.  We prepare posters and practice our 15 minute research presentations.  We practice reading and sharing our scholarly endeavors through other media.  But we have not typically been provided with opportunites to learn and therefore we are not as skilled in communicating our scholarship to others outside our discipline and especially not to the public in general.</p>
<p>Enter actor Alan Alda and &#8220;communicating science&#8221;.   On the occasion of the annual meeting of the Council of Graduate Schools in 2010, attendees had the opportunity to learn about and experience the <a href="http:/http://www.centerforcommunicatingscience.org/">Communicating Science</a> initiative offered through the <a href="http://vimeo.com/50792901">center</a> at Stony Brook University.  Many of us were in agreement that the initiative is a powerful program that helps &#8220;scientists&#8221; and scholars to develop communication skills.</p>
<p><img data-attachment-id="482" data-permalink="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/kpdtge/index.php/2013/01/13/communicating-scholarly-endeavors/img_2718/" data-orig-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2013/01/IMG_2718.jpg" data-orig-size="2592,1944" data-comments-opened="1" data-image-meta="{&quot;aperture&quot;:&quot;2.8&quot;,&quot;credit&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;camera&quot;:&quot;Canon PowerShot G10&quot;,&quot;caption&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;created_timestamp&quot;:&quot;1291397946&quot;,&quot;copyright&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;focal_length&quot;:&quot;6.1&quot;,&quot;iso&quot;:&quot;200&quot;,&quot;shutter_speed&quot;:&quot;0.016666666666667&quot;,&quot;title&quot;:&quot;&quot;}" data-image-title="IMG_2718" data-image-description="" data-medium-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2013/01/IMG_2718-300x225.jpg" data-large-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2013/01/IMG_2718-1024x768.jpg" class="alignnone size-thumbnail wp-image-482" style="color: inherit;font: normal normal normal 15px/normal 'Helvetica Neue', Helvetica, Arial, sans-serif;font-style: inherit;font-weight: inherit;line-height: 1.625;border-style: solid;border-color: #dddddd;cursor: default;margin-top: 0.4em;height: auto;border-width: 1px;padding: 6px" src="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2013/01/IMG_2718-e1358116460245-139x150.jpg" alt="" width="139" height="150" /></p>
<p>Realizing the importance of this work, Virginia Tech has embraced &#8220;communicating science&#8221; and initiated a program here.  Within the context of the Transformative Graduate Education (TGE) initiative of the VT Graduate School, Professor Patty Raun from the Theatre Department has offered sessions within GRAD 5104 Preparing the Future Professoriate graduate course and has established a new graduate course on Communicating Science.  Through this program and others we will engage VT graduate students with the social responsibility of the university to communicate science.</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>


		<p>The global perspectives program (GPP) for 2013 has begun!   The GPP Switzerland blog is coming back to life filled with new voices, reflections and shared anticipations.  There will be more posts, tweets, and connections to come.  The VT participants are coming together and will soon be connected with UniBasel participants.</p>
<p>Fourteen individuals from Virginia Tech were selected from six colleges (Agriculture &amp; Life Sciences, Architecture &amp; Urban Studies, Engineering, Liberal Arts &amp; Human Sciences, Sciences, Veterinary Medicine).  The monthly meetings and interactions among us are well underway.  We&#8217;re getting to know each other and starting to develop a sense of the group. And of course, I&#8217;ve already fallen into my habit of counting heads (to make sure we&#8217;re all here).</p>
<p>Our itinerary filled with university visits and cultural experiences is nearly complete.  Plane tickets are being purchased; individual travel plans are created.  Hotel reservations are made.  Our meals at fine restaurants are booked &#8211; we will dine very well.  The train trips especially through the Swiss Alps are almost finalized.  Our global perspectives program is taking shape &#8211; a choreography in the making.  Keep watching and reading as the story continues and the journey unfolds.</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>


		<p>I&#8217;ve been thinking a lot about the World Wide Web and social media and their impact and utility in graduate schools.  Conceptually I have understood the functionality associated with Web 1.0 and 2.0 and have sought to utilize these phases for enhanced digital interaction and communication.  What follows are some of my initial musings.</p>
<p>As a 21st century institution, the VT graduate school has undergone a transition from the traditional role as an administrative office to ‘a place and space for graduate education.’  Throughout the last century, graduate schools (not unlike other institutions) tended to operate as “top down” offices providing information (policy, procedures) through “static” means (catalogs, manuals) to “users” (constituencies, especially students) as the receivers of information.  Words similar to these have been used to describe the early days of the World Wide Web (<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Web_1.0">1.0</a>) &#8211; users could only view (receive) information and not contribute to the &#8220;webpages&#8221;, users (constituencies) as consumers of content not active participants, and the information wasn&#8217;t dynamic.  Although available since 1993, the use of web technology by graduate schools began in earnest mostly in the 21<sup>st</sup> century and reflected the Web 1.0 approach of delivery to consumers.  We took what we did and delivered it electronically.</p>
<p>The onset of <a href="http://oreilly.com/pub/a/web2/archive/what-is-web-20.html">Web 2.0</a> in 2002 and the availability of interactive tools and social media ultimately challenged graduate schools (as well as universities and national associations) to examine our operations and to embrace the change which was well underway.  Web 2.0 allows for uses beyond the static delivery of content.  It allows users to generate, interact and collaborate in virtual community.  Web 2.0 tools include wikis, blogs, and numerous social networking sites.  The VT Graduate School was one of the first in the nation to move to Web 2.0 conceptually and to build interactive tools (e.g., <a href="http://graduateschool.vt.edu/graduate_catalog/">on-line catalog</a>, <a href="http://graduateschool.vt.edu/">featured graduate student</a>, <a href="http://graduateschool.vt.edu/">upcoming examinations)</a> and to embrace social media (<a href="https://secure.graduateschool.vt.edu/weblog/monthlymemo/">blogs</a>, <a href="https://twitter.com/VTGradCommunity">Twitter, </a><a href="https://www.facebook.com/pages/Virginia-Tech-Graduate-School/348802603577">Facebook</a>), all of which are inter-connected on the <a href="http://graduateschool.vt.edu/">Graduate School website</a>.   These examples and the development of the virtual GLC (<a href="http://openlife01.cc.vt.edu:9500/wifi/">vGLC</a>) are still works in progress and ones that draw upon the greater interactivity of Web 2.0.</p>
<p>Today graduate schools must actively encourage sharing of information, the creation on content, and collaboration among the constituencies.  Although there is some &#8220;content to be delivered&#8221; the message and tools of Web 2.0 challenges graduate schools to think differently about what we do and how we do it and I&#8217;m not just referring to the administrative functions but the whole of the graduate education.  Using web technologies, graduate schools (2.0+) must rethink graduate education, embrace change and redefine &#8220;space and place&#8221; to include the brick and mortar of the physical space as well as the digital space and build graduate community.</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>


		<p>Recent conversations at multiple venues have prompted me to reflect on Wilhelm von Humboldt, the Doctor of Philosophy degree and the evolution of the modern research university.  First, the Swiss higher education system and the routine acknowledgement of Humboldt &#8216;s influence were discussed during the <a href="http://internationalaffairs.unibas.ch/print/joint-programmes/joint-visiting-programmes/gpp/gpp-input-seminar/">Input Seminar</a> for the UniBasel Global Perspectives Program (March 18-19, 2013).  Two days later in Dublin at the <a href="http://www.eua.be/eua-membership-and-services/Home.aspx">EUA-CDE </a>Global Forum on Doctoral Education (March 20-22), the topics of conversation included the evolution of doctoral education in Europe and the increasing emphasis on research in doctoral education (PhD).  During the last few class sessions of GRAD 5104 Preparing the Future Professoriate, our discussions focused on global higher education and several international students shared an overview of the higher education systems from their home countries.  This provided the opportunities to reflect on the historical perspective of higher education and their influences on universities around the world.  At the March meeting of the 2013 VT <a href="http://graduateschool.vt.edu/graduate_school/gpp/">Global Perspectives Program</a>, we discussed terminology and the similarities and differences to understand better the evolution of global higher education and the universities that we will visit in May.  And finally as I read through selected blog posts from <a href="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/grad5104">GRAD 5104</a> and <a href="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/pfpswitzerland/">GPP Switzerland</a> I pondered the themes of these &#8216;conversations&#8217; and realized the underlying but un-articulated interconnectedness of the 21st century research university, the evolution of the PhD and the influence of Humboldt.  Humboldt, the German university and the man, are frequently referenced in discussions about the university in Europe but less so in the United States although his influence is part of U.S. history as well.</p>
<p><img alt="WilhelmvonHumboldt.jpg" src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/af/WilhelmvonHumboldt.jpg" width="194" height="236" /></p>
<p><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wilhelm_von_Humboldt">Wilhelm von Humboldt</a> (1767-1835) was a 19th century philosopher, a Prussian diplomat, and an early &#8216;architect&#8217; of national education including the &#8216;university&#8217;.  He is well known throughout Europe as the founder of the University of Berlin in 1810. Within a relatively short period of time, the University of Berlin (Humboldt) would soon became a model for 19th century European universities and ultimately would influence the development of U.S. universities. In recognition of Humboldt&#8217;s influence in shaping the modern university, the university was renamed <a href="http://www.hu-berlin.de/">Humboldt University of Berlin </a>in 1949. The name of Humboldt reflects both Wilhelm&#8217;s contributions and those of his brother Alexander, a famous geographer and explorer.</p>
<p>Wilhelm von Humboldt espoused the view that the university should be a community of scholars and students.  In this <a href="http://www.historyandpolicy.org/papers/policy-paper-98.html#S4">&#8216;Humboldtian&#8217; university, </a>teaching and research were interconnected and vital to the work of the individual scholar.  Although important to advancement of knowledge and integral to the university&#8217;s mission, research was thought by Humboldt to be &#8216;ancillary to teaching&#8217;.  This notion persisted until the 20th century when research would finally be recognized as a &#8216;vital entity in itself&#8217; thus setting the stage for the further development and prominence of the modern research university.</p>
<p>During the recent European University Association (EUA) &#8211; Council on Doctoral Education (<a href="http://www.eua.be/cde/about-euacde.aspx">CDE</a>) <a href="https://twitter.com/euatweets/status/314736768450703361">Global Strategic Forum on Doctoral Education</a> in Dublin, the PhD was described historically as a &#8216;license to teach&#8217;.  This makes sense when one considers that the original purpose was to prepare scholars to teach in universities.  For many years, the doctoral degree required advanced scholarship but not original research.  And once Wilhelm von Humboldt entered the discussions, the strong link between teaching and research was made that would change the university.  As the value of research expanded and the desire for original research increased throughout the last century, the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Doctor_of_Philosophy">Doctor of Philosophy</a> degree changed and the PhD is recognized as a research degree worldwide.</p>
<p>The modern research university will continue to evolve and an emphasis on research will remain.  But the conversations about doctoral education must also continue about the importance of teaching and learning, the preparation for careers outside higher education, and the engagement between the university and society.  These conversations are happening within EUA-CDE regularly and will continue in the Future Professoriate graduate course (GRAD 5104) and especially the VT-UniBasel Global Perspectives Program.  I look forward to the ongoing dialogue.</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
