<p>&nbsp;</p>


		<p><a href="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2013/05/3619162.jpg"><img data-attachment-id="602" data-permalink="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/kpdtge/index.php/2013/05/22/on-the-eve-of-departure-once-again/attachment/3619162/" data-orig-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2013/05/3619162.jpg" data-orig-size="335,500" data-comments-opened="1" data-image-meta="{&quot;aperture&quot;:&quot;6.3&quot;,&quot;credit&quot;:&quot;Picasa 2.7&quot;,&quot;camera&quot;:&quot;NIKON D200&quot;,&quot;caption&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;created_timestamp&quot;:&quot;1185799582&quot;,&quot;copyright&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;focal_length&quot;:&quot;18&quot;,&quot;iso&quot;:&quot;100&quot;,&quot;shutter_speed&quot;:&quot;0.00625&quot;,&quot;title&quot;:&quot;&quot;}" data-image-title="3619162" data-image-description="" data-medium-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2013/05/3619162-201x300.jpg" data-large-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2013/05/3619162.jpg" class="alignright size-thumbnail wp-image-602" alt="3619162" src="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2013/05/3619162-150x150.jpg" width="150" height="150" /></a>Eight years ago in May 2006 my bags were packed and I boarded a plane to meet the 10 Virginia Tech graduate students at the Hotel St. Josef in Zurich, Switzerland selected to participate in the launch of the global perspectives portion of the preparing the future professoriate program.  As it is now called, the <a title="Global Perspectives Program" href="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/globalperspectives/">Global Perspectives Program</a> is offered within the <a href="http://graduateschool.vt.edu/graduate_school/tge/index.html">Transformative Graduate Education</a> initiative developed by the Virginia Tech Graduate School.</p>
<p><img data-attachment-id="594" data-permalink="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/kpdtge/on_the_balcony/" data-orig-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2013/05/on_the_balcony.jpg" data-orig-size="800,600" data-comments-opened="1" data-image-meta="{&quot;aperture&quot;:&quot;7.1&quot;,&quot;credit&quot;:&quot;Picasa 3.0&quot;,&quot;camera&quot;:&quot;DSC-P200&quot;,&quot;caption&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;created_timestamp&quot;:&quot;1148885582&quot;,&quot;copyright&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;focal_length&quot;:&quot;13.7&quot;,&quot;iso&quot;:&quot;100&quot;,&quot;shutter_speed&quot;:&quot;0.008&quot;,&quot;title&quot;:&quot;&quot;}" data-image-title="on_the_balcony" data-image-description="" data-medium-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2013/05/on_the_balcony-300x225.jpg" data-large-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2013/05/on_the_balcony.jpg" class="alignleft size-thumbnail wp-image-594" alt="on_the_balcony" src="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2013/05/on_the_balcony-150x150.jpg" width="150" height="150" /></p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<p><em>First GPP at VT Center for European Studies and Architecture, Riva San Vitale, 2006.</em></p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<p>It is now May 2013 and another eve of departure has arrived.  In the past 8 years, there have been many eves of departure &#8211; nine to be exact and this will be the tenth.  Most of the departures were for the GPP experience focused primarily in <a href="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/pfpswitzerland/">Switzerland</a>, one was for the pilot <a href="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/pfpchile/">GPP in Chile</a>, and one was for the <a href="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/gpgraddeans/">Global Perspectives program for graduate deans</a>.</p>
<p>So much has happened since that first departure.  What began as simply an idea, a possibility, has become a <a href="http://www.vtnews.vt.edu/articles/2010/09/092710-outreach-globalperspectives.html">reality</a>.  A partnership has been forged with the <a href="http://internationalaffairs.unibas.ch/joint-programmes/joint-visiting-programmes/gpp/">University of Basel.  </a>Graduate deans from other U.S. graduate schools are watching what we do and are developing global perspectives programs designed for their home institutions.  And I continue to consider possibilities for expanding the program.  By many measures the GPP has been a success: 120+ participants and multiple visits to universities in Switzerland, Italy, France, and Germany.  Presentations and publications.  Strong connections across universities and among academic leaders.  Alumni.  Collaborations.  And more.</p>
<p>The global perspectives program is more than study abroad although it probably falls under the category so identified by the university and described by colleagues.  It isn&#8217;t just a program, it is an experience and yes, an experience not unlike a study abroad program but yet somehow it is different.  It involves graduate students &#8211; that&#8217;s somewhat unique. The graduate students come from different disciplines &#8211; that&#8217;s unique.  The program is offered by a graduate school and led by a graduate dean &#8211; that&#8217;s definitely unique. The graduate students&#8217; projects are unrelated to their research.  We visit universities to understand more about global higher education &#8211; we meet with academic leadership, faculty and students, we visit different academic units (faculties, departments, buildings).  We contribute individually and collectively to knowledge and understanding of a shared theme &#8211; this year &#8216;university &amp; society: meeting expectations?.&#8217;  We interact across disciplinary perspectives, we reach across cultures and languages.  We learn.  We appreciate.  And we learn to appreciate.</p>
<p>GPP is also about the opportunity for participants to learn more about themselves.   And the experience can be a very personal one.  Sometimes it happens unknowingly, sometimes reluctantly and sometimes quite willingly.</p>
<p>As a part of the experience, I ask the Virginia Tech participants to keep journals and to write about their observations and personal reflections.  As I challenge myself daily, I encourage the GPPers to see new things and to see things in new ways (e.g., the <a href="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/kpdtge/index.php/2012/05/14/on-the-eve-of-departure/">doors and windows</a>) and to see the <a title="Seeing the unobvious: Fed Ex and other visuals" href="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/kpdtge/index.php/2012/10/24/seeing-the-unobvious-fed-ex-and-other-visuals/">unobvious</a>.</p>
<p>Once again I am on the eve of departure and looking forward to another wonderful shared experience and an individual journey.</p>


		<p>The summer solstice occurred on the same day as the final event of the 2013 Global Perspectives Program (GPP).  Summer solstice &#8211; the longest day of the year- is a celebration of (day)light and this year in particular of solar energy and Switzerland.</p>
<p>On Friday June 21st, the Swiss Embassy in Washington DC once again opened its doors to host the 2013 Global Perspectives Conference on Preparing Future Academic Leaders.  The theme this year was &#8220;University and society: Meeting expectations?&#8221;.  VT GPP program and UniBasel GPP participants served as panelists and discussion leaders for the intriguing dialogue focused on the expectations of the university, society and students and raising more questions than answers about the purpose of a university, the value of an education and the future of higher education.<a href="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2013/06/IMG_6623.jpg"><img data-attachment-id="671" data-permalink="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/kpdtge/index.php/2013/06/24/summer-solstice-solar-impulse-and-swiss-embassy/img_6623/" data-orig-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2013/06/IMG_6623.jpg" data-orig-size="3456,2592" data-comments-opened="1" data-image-meta="{&quot;aperture&quot;:&quot;2.8&quot;,&quot;credit&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;camera&quot;:&quot;Canon PowerShot G10&quot;,&quot;caption&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;created_timestamp&quot;:&quot;1371824727&quot;,&quot;copyright&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;focal_length&quot;:&quot;6.1&quot;,&quot;iso&quot;:&quot;200&quot;,&quot;shutter_speed&quot;:&quot;0.016666666666667&quot;,&quot;title&quot;:&quot;&quot;}" data-image-title="IMG_6623" data-image-description="" data-medium-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2013/06/IMG_6623-300x225.jpg" data-large-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2013/06/IMG_6623-1024x768.jpg" class="alignleft size-thumbnail wp-image-671" alt="IMG_6623" src="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2013/06/IMG_6623-150x150.jpg" width="150" height="150" /></a></p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<p>The evening before, the Swiss Ambassador Manuel Sager <a href="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2013/06/IMG_6579.jpg"><img data-attachment-id="674" data-permalink="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/kpdtge/index.php/2013/06/24/summer-solstice-solar-impulse-and-swiss-embassy/img_6579/" data-orig-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2013/06/IMG_6579.jpg" data-orig-size="3456,2592" data-comments-opened="1" data-image-meta="{&quot;aperture&quot;:&quot;4.5&quot;,&quot;credit&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;camera&quot;:&quot;Canon PowerShot G10&quot;,&quot;caption&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;created_timestamp&quot;:&quot;1371762891&quot;,&quot;copyright&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;focal_length&quot;:&quot;30.5&quot;,&quot;iso&quot;:&quot;400&quot;,&quot;shutter_speed&quot;:&quot;0.125&quot;,&quot;title&quot;:&quot;&quot;}" data-image-title="IMG_6579" data-image-description="" data-medium-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2013/06/IMG_6579-300x225.jpg" data-large-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2013/06/IMG_6579-1024x768.jpg" class="alignleft size-thumbnail wp-image-674" alt="IMG_6579" src="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2013/06/IMG_6579-150x150.jpg" width="150" height="150" /></a>hosted a reception at the Air &amp; Space Museum (Smithsonian) to celebrate the success of the<a href="http://www.solarimpulse.com/"> Solar Impulse</a> project and the <a href="http://www.solarimpulse.com/en/airplane/">plane</a> the size of a 747 flown across the U.S using only solar energy.  The pilots, Andre and Bertrand, received <a href="http://www.solarimpulse.com/en/">2013  Spirit Award </a>from Charles A. and Anne Morrow Lindbergh  Foundation.</p>
<p>At the same time of our events were underway in Washington, a group of graduate deans were engaged in discussions of global graduate education in Germany as part of the DAAD study tour.  One of these deans (Nancy Marcus, FSU) was a participant in the <a href="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/gpgraddeans/">2012 graduate deans global perspectives</a> program offered by K. DePauw.  To read more about the Germany study tour and the graduate deans program, please read the <a href="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/globalperspectives/">Global Perspectives blog</a> into which these posts flow.</p>
<p>With inspiration and some sadness, the GPP&#8217;13 experience has ended but happily the participants have entered into the next phase &#8211; as GPP alumni.  The GPP manual and papers will be written and available <a href="http://internationalaffairs.unibas.ch/joint-programmes/joint-visiting-programmes/gpp/">electronically</a>.  I anticipate that the conversations to continue via twitter (<a href="https://twitter.com/gppvt">gppvt</a>, #gpp13, @GPPUniBasel ), facebook (<a href="https://www.facebook.com/GlobalPerspectivesProgramVirginiaTech">GPPVT Facebook</a>, <a href="https://www.facebook.com/pages/Global-Perspectives-Programme-University-of-Basel/119816501460078">UniBasel</a>), LinkedIn (GPP alumni), blogs, google + and more.  Our lives have been enriched by the GPP experiences and we continue to grow as global scholars and colleagues.</p>
<p>Thanks all for another wonderful adventure &#8211; a quest for sure!</p>


		<p>A week has passed since our adventure called global perspectives program began in Zurich.  We have traveled by plane, train, bus, tram, and foot on our wonderful journey. Our university visits have been completed with the final two completed on Monday &#8211; <a href="http://www.usi.ch/en/index.htm">USI</a> and <a href="http://www.supsi.ch/home_en.html">SUPSI</a>.</p>
<p>We have blogged, tweeted, and posted on FaceBook.  We have briefed, debriefed, chatted and debated on the train, over meals, in the garden and beyond.  The VT GPP&#8217;13 could talk (and some probably do) into the wee hours of the night.</p>
<p>And now we await the arrival of our colleagues from the University of Basel GPP&#8217;13 program.  The theme of our global seminar is &#8220;Universities and Society: Meeting expectations?&#8221;  The dialogue will undoubtedly be rich and informative.  The public version will be shared at the Embassy of Switzerland in Washington, DC on June 19, 2013.</p>


		<p>One of the highlights of the Global Perspectives Program is the opportunity to meet and engage in active dialogue with the presidents (rectors) of selected universities:  President Prof. Dr. Andreas Fischer of the University of Zurich, Rector Prof. Dr. Antonio Loprieno of the University of Basel, President Prof. Dr. M. Alain Beretz of the University of Strasbourg and President Piero Martinoli, University of Lugano (Università della Svizzera italiana or USI).  The conversations were informative about the future of global higher education.  The individuals are quite inspiring and very accessible.</p>
<p><img data-attachment-id="626" data-permalink="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/kpdtge/index.php/2013/06/02/visits-with-university-presidents/fischer_andreas/" data-orig-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2013/06/Fischer_Andreas.jpg" data-orig-size="170,235" data-comments-opened="1" data-image-meta="{&quot;aperture&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;credit&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;camera&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;caption&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;created_timestamp&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;copyright&quot;:&quot;UZH; Frank Br\u00fcderli&quot;,&quot;focal_length&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;iso&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;shutter_speed&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;title&quot;:&quot;&quot;}" data-image-title="Fischer_Andreas" data-image-description="" data-medium-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2013/06/Fischer_Andreas.jpg" data-large-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2013/06/Fischer_Andreas.jpg" class="alignleft size-thumbnail wp-image-626" style="color: inherit;font: normal normal normal 15px/normal 'Helvetica Neue', Helvetica, Arial, sans-serif;font-style: inherit;font-weight: inherit;line-height: 1.625;border-style: solid;border-color: #dddddd;cursor: default;float: left;margin-right: 1.625em;height: auto;margin-bottom: 1.625em;border-width: 1px;padding: 6px" alt="Fischer_Andreas" src="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2013/06/Fischer_Andreas-150x150.jpg" width="150" height="150" /></p>

<p>In 1985 Andreas Fischer was appointed full professor of English philology at the University of Zurich. From 2004 to 2006 he served as Dean of the Faculty of Arts and from 2006 until his appointment as President of the University of Zurich in 2008 he acted as Vice President for Arts and Social Sciences.  Fischer will retire this year and his replacement is currently in progress.</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<p><a href="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2013/06/F4C7683DC09F28B634E8A9B44A01A12F.0.jpg"><img data-attachment-id="624" data-permalink="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/kpdtge/index.php/2013/06/02/visits-with-university-presidents/f4c7683dc09f28b634e8a9b44a01a12f-0/" data-orig-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2013/06/F4C7683DC09F28B634E8A9B44A01A12F.0.jpg" data-orig-size="90,135" data-comments-opened="1" data-image-meta="{&quot;aperture&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;credit&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;camera&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;caption&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;created_timestamp&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;copyright&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;focal_length&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;iso&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;shutter_speed&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;title&quot;:&quot;&quot;}" data-image-title="F4C7683DC09F28B634E8A9B44A01A12F.0" data-image-description="" data-medium-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2013/06/F4C7683DC09F28B634E8A9B44A01A12F.0.jpg" data-large-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2013/06/F4C7683DC09F28B634E8A9B44A01A12F.0.jpg" class="alignleft size-full wp-image-624" alt="F4C7683DC09F28B634E8A9B44A01A12F.0" src="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2013/06/F4C7683DC09F28B634E8A9B44A01A12F.0.jpg" width="90" height="135" /></a></p>
<p>Professor Loprieno has been Full Professor of Egyptology at the University of Basel since the year 2000 and was recently reappointed to an unprecedented third term as Rector. His main research areas include Near Eastern languages and Egyptian cultural history and religion. Prior to his appointment as Rector of the University of Basel, he served as Dean of Studies of the University’s Faculty of Humanities. He heads the Conference of Swiss University Rectors (CRUS).</p>

<p>Professor Piero Martinoli is president of the University of Italian Switzerland in Lugano where he has served since September of 2006.  He studied at the ETH Zurich, where he obtained a degree in physics, and his doctorate in physics.</p>

<p>These individuals have graciously greeted the GPP participants and the Global Perspectives Graduate Deans programs throughout their tenure as President.  We have benefited from their expertise and willingness to spend time with us!</p>
</div>


		<p>The language that we use is important especially the words and what they imply.  We know this and we can cite many different examples.  I will offer only one perspective that resulted from my readings about faculty in higher education recently.  Not surprisingly, I regularly read the <a href="http://chronicle.com/section/Home/5">Chronicle of Higher Education</a>, <a href="http://www.insidehighered.com/">Inside Higher Education</a> and other similar venues about higher education.  My comments which follow are not a criticism of these publications but should be viewed as a commentary about how we in the academy continue to use familiar words and phrases that while accurately portraying a current situation do therefore perpetuate these notions as if they are &#8220;fact&#8221; and can&#8217;t be changed in the future. Two examples follow.</p>
<p>The first of two phrases that I read and hear colleagues use is the &#8220;two body problem&#8221;.  These words are commonly used to describe the situation in which two individuals (e.g., spouses or partners), or at least one of these individuals, seek faculty positions in higher education.  Since the 1980s, words like spousal hire, partner accommodation, and more recently dual career hires have been used.  An underlying assumption was that this was a &#8220;challenge&#8221; or &#8220;problem&#8221;.  I agree (and have argued favorably on numerous occasions) that indeed higher education needed to become aware of and proactively address the fact that increasingly so couples desire career opportunities for each individual and therefore, often two faculty positions. This phenomenon has increased over time and has become a reality facing higher education.  And thus rather than call it &#8220;the two body problem&#8221; which immediately casts the situation negatively as a problem, perhaps we could use language that reflects a positive attitude and encourages action.  The message sent and received is very different if we change &#8220;problem&#8221; to &#8220;opportunity&#8221;. <em> Inside Higher Education</em> has made positive strides forward in this arena through the featuring &#8220;dual career&#8221; couples (reflecting via photos a full range of diversity) and their opportunities to <a href="http:/http://careers.insidehighered.com/search">seek dual careers</a> as evident on their <a href="http://www.insidehighered.com/">website</a>.  This sends a message that two careers are possible rather than a problem.</p>
<p>The second phrase and one that is relatively new is &#8220;<a href="http://chronicle.com/article/The-Baby-Penalty/140813/">the baby penalty</a>&#8220;.  Dr. Mason (former Graduate Dean at UC Berkeley and current faculty member) and her colleagues have studied and authored a recent book in an attempt to answer the question of whether or not babies matter.  Their research shows that babies do matter and make a difference in the lives of female academics.  Honestly, I don&#8217;t find this surprising because I think intuitively we know that having babies and raising children does impact one&#8217;s lives and more so for females than the males.   While the data do support a &#8220;negative&#8221; impact upon the female faculty member in a traditional sense of academy, the data are also a reflection of the way higher education is currently structured and not the way that it could be.  Families and babies should not be referred to as a &#8220;penalty&#8221;.  In the <a href="http://chronicle.com/article/The-Baby-Penalty/140813/">Chronicle of Higher Education article</a>, Mason stated that it is time to &#8220;&#8230; demand family policies that will at least give them a fighting chance to have both a successful career and babies.&#8221;  University leaders could use the data to insist that higher education actually make structural changes and more fully embrace families and work-life balance in our colleges and universities.  This truly is an opportunity and perhaps a mandate for change.  Let&#8217;s begin by modifying our words because language is important.</p>
<div></div>
<p>&nbsp;</p>


		<p><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Donald_Kennedy">Donald Kennedy</a>, President Emeritus of Stanford University wrote about the responsibilities of faculty in his book entitled <a href="http://books.google.com/books?id=1BOUGMw_mgwC&amp;printsec=frontcover&amp;source=gbs_ge_summary_r&amp;cad=0#v=onepage&amp;q&amp;f=false">Academic Duty</a> (1997).  Kennedy wrote that &#8220;<a href="http://www.aaup.org/issues/academic-freedom">academic freedom</a>&#8221; was well known but less so &#8220;academic duty&#8221; due to the &#8220;relatively uncodified&#8221; (p. vii) understanding of faculty work. He argued that faculty work included the following duties: to teach, to mentor, to serve the university, to discover, to publish, to tell the truth, to reach beyond the walls, and to change. Today, we would likely propose that the work of faculty has expanded to include additional roles including grant writing, fundraising, public relations, global perspective, civility, and building inclusive communities to name a few.</p>
<p>As I reflect on these duties, I think we could agree that many of the academic duties (e.g., to teach, to discover, to publish, to serve, to mentor) are well known and accepted among the responsibilities of faculty.  The degree to which these and other duties are evident in the lives of the faculty do vary some depending upon the type of university and type of faculty position but they are what we can expect when hired as a tenure track faculty member.  But they do represent the core of faculty work.</p>
<p>Two of the duties deserve additional comment &#8211; &#8220;to tell the truth&#8221; and &#8220;to change&#8221;. The academic duty of &#8220;to tell the truth&#8221; has become increasingly more important especially in the context of almost daily reports of research misconduct, plagiarism, and other examples of lapses in professional and scholarly ethics in higher education.  The availability of entities such as the Office of Research Integrity (<a href="http://ori.dhhs.gov/">ORI</a>) with the Department of Health and Human Services (DHHS) and official ethics guidelines and training programs through National Science Foundation (<a href="http://www.nsf.gov/bfa/dias/policy/rcr.jsp">NSF</a>) and National Institutes of Health (<a href="http://ethics.od.nih.gov/">NIH</a>) have brought greater attention to and scrutiny of scholars and their scholarly work and sometimes professional and even personal lives.  Online academic news sources especially the <a href="http://chronicle.com/section/Home/5">Chronicle of Higher Education</a>, <a href="http://www.insidehighered.com/">Inside Higher Education</a>, <a href="http://www.universityworldnews.com/">World University News</a> regularly provide news and updates on cases of academic misconduct and of course, social media including twitter (e.g., <a href="https://twitter.com/HHS_ORI">ORI twitter</a>) helps disseminate information.  Professional codes of conduct and ethical guidelines exist in many academic disciplines and are often incorporated into the professional development of the future faculty.</p>
<p>&#8220;To change&#8221; is the second academic duty to be highlighted here.  It has been said that universities are slow to change and those of us who have been in higher education for some time would likely agree.  But I would argue that universities and therefore faculty have a responsibility to change, to grow and to challenge ourselves to continue to be meaningful and relevant today and for the future.  Universities are social institutions and therefore have a responsibility to society, including a global society.  Higher education has been challenged by the technological advancements and the rapid rate of change. One need only to consider the development of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/World_Wide_Web">internet</a> and the surprising speed of the <a href="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/kpdtge/index.php/2013/03/09/graduate-education-and-web-2-0/">transition </a>from Web 1.0 to <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Web_2.0">2.0</a> and the most recent development of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Massive_open_online_course">MOOC </a>and its impact upon higher education. Institutions of higher learning have yet to realize the full extent of these developments. If we are open to it, MOOCs will help us understand more about learners and learning and they can challenge us to think differently about how we provide opportunities for acquiring and disseminate knowledge.  These are but two examples about how we must engage with change and prepare the faculty (and future faculty) to change and to be changed.</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>


		<p>In the early 1990s a colleague used the word &#8220;futurisktic&#8221; or at least that&#8217;s what I thought I heard. (Note: I tried to give him credit years ago but he claims not to have coined the word).  Anyway, I was intrigued by the word that cleverly combined future and risk and I quickly adopted it for use in my musings about the future and in some of my presentations and publications.</p>
<p><a href="http://www.sethgodin.com/sg/">Seth Godin</a> wrote a recent <a href="http://sethgodin.typepad.com/seths_blog/2013/11/every-presentation-worth-doing-has-just-one-purpose.html">blog</a> in which he argued that &#8220;every presentation worth doing has just one purpose&#8221; and that is to make change happen.  According to Godin, &#8220;change, of course, opens doors, it creates possibilities and it&#8217;s fraught with danger and apparent risk.   Much easier to deny this than it is to embrace it.&#8221;  Godin&#8217;s advice seems to fit with what I identify as futurisktic.</p>
<p>As a concept, &#8220;futurisktic&#8221; implies keeping an eye to and a vision for the future with attention to the opportunities and challenges (risks) associated with progress.  Being futurisktic is about change.  It is about embracing risk as an integral aspect of change.  Risk should not be viewed as a negative but risk taking will likely force us out of our comfort zones.  By doing so, it allows us to acknowledge and embrace the meaningfulness and value of change.  I&#8217;m not arguing for change simply for change sake or simply taking risks without thought.  Being futurisktic is about pushing ourselves and pushing the limits as is so wonderfully exemplified in the video entitled <a href="http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=rDiUVS_-4_Q">the future is ours</a>.</p>
<p>Another example among many is the TED talk by Sir Ken Robinson about the<a href="http://www.ted.com/talks/sir_ken_robinson_bring_on_the_revolution.html"> learning revolution</a> in which he describes the need for revolution not evolution. To actively engage in transformation and to acknowledge that often what stops us from making progress is the &#8220;tyranny of common sense.&#8221;  Robinson argues for and encourages us to become active participants in the learning revolution.</p>
<p>These examples are but a few of those that illustrate futurisktic endeavors.  They provide examples of intentional, purposeful and meaningful change &#8211; growth, progress and advancement.  We live in a time of rapid change.  As we engage change, I encourage us to be futuristic in our thinking with a willingness to take risks &#8211; that is, futurisktic!</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>


		<p>When asked by <a href="http://aminotes.tumblr.com/post/4994624354/isaac-asimov-predicted-the-internet-of-today-20">Bill Moyers in April 1988</a> &#8220;can we have a revolution in learning?&#8221;, Issac Asimov responded with “Yes, I think not only that we can but that we must.&#8221;  He went on to talk about the time when &#8220;<strong>once we have computer outlets in every home, each of them hooked up to enormous libraries, where you can ask any question and be given answers, you can look up something you’re interested in knowing&#8221;. </strong> Asimov was actually talking about the internet before the internet had become a reality.  And he was talking about the need for educational reform and the need for lifelong learning, learning that was individualized, a learning revolution some 35 years ago.  His message of the late 1980s is as important today as it was then.</p>

&#8220;That’s another trouble with education as we now have it. People think of education as something that they can finish.&#8221; &#8212; Isaac Asimov<br />
<a href="https://twitter.com/kpdepauw/status/417326401831043072">12/29/13 10:01 AM</a></td>

<p>Did we have a learning revolution in the late 20th century?  Perhaps we could say that some progress has been made but with more needed (that&#8217;s for a longer conversation). Although there are many good things happening in learning in schools and higher education, we have yet to realize the full extent and possibilities of the learning revolution.  Sir Kenneth Robinson, in arguing for the <a href="http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=r9LelXa3U_I">learning revolution</a> states emphatically that what is needed is a transformation from the &#8220;dogmas of the past&#8221; and the &#8220;tyranny of common sense&#8221;.  In a <a href="http://www.ted.com/talks/ken_robinson_how_to_escape_education_s_death_valley.html">2013 TED talk</a>, Robinson spoke eloquently about the three principles &#8220;crucial for the human mind to flourish&#8221;, creativity and the climate of possibility for education in the U.S.</p>
<p>Robinson and Asimov are but two of the scholars who have argued for educational reform. Although they have often focused on public school education, their messages are very compelling and therefore applicable for higher education as well.  The learning revolution can and should occur at colleges and universities in the United States and around the world.  Some examples are currently underway within the Commonwealth of Virginia including the <a href="http://www.provost.vcu.edu/viceprovosts/innovation_studentsuccess.html">Office of the Vice Provost for Learning Innovation and Student Success</a> as VCU, the Technology-enhanced Learning and OnLine Strategies (<a href="http://www.tlos.vt.edu/">TLOS</a>) at Virginia Tech, and the Transformative Graduate Education (<a href="http://graduateschool.vt.edu/Transformative-Graduate-Education">TGE</a>) initiative offered by the VT Graduate School.</p>
<p>Let&#8217;s encourage our colleagues to engage our students (undergraduate, graduate), faculty and administrators in conversations about transformation and change for universities for the 21st century and to lead the learning revolution.</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>


		<div>
<p>Some years ago now, a colleague of mine wrote about conditions for graduate study which not only influenced my thinking in the 1980s but still resonate today.  I have recalled these conditions and used to share them often with others especially new graduate students. But hadn&#8217;t thought much about their influence until recently when I was reflecting on the topic of this blog post.</p>
<p>William Harper (1980) wrote &#8220;Some conditions for graduate study&#8221; which was published in the academic journal entitled <em>Quest</em> (Vol. 32 Issue 2, p174).  Harper argued that there were at least four conditions for graduate study: academic quality, time to fiddle, a baggy idea of truth, and a sense of community.  The words themselves seem readily apparent in meaning and informative of that which should be a part of graduate education.</p>
<p>As a graduate dean, I&#8217;m often in a position to welcome incoming graduate students and encourage them along the journey through graduate study.  I have used words such as expect academic rigor and quality, demand excellence and to hold high expectations of oneself and others.  I have encouraged graduate students to work hard but to play as well (hopefully keeping a balance). I have been and continue to be a strong advocate for building a diverse and inclusive graduate education community.  In reflecting upon these phrases, there is a definite connection back to Harper&#8217;s writings for which I am grateful and wish to acknowledge.</p>
<p>So what about doodles?  According to Wikipedia, a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Doodle"><b>doodle</b></a> &#8220;is an unfocused or unconscious drawing made while a person&#8217;s attention is otherwise occupied. Doodles are simple drawings that can have concrete representational meaning or may just be abstract shapes&#8221;.  The <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Doodle">Wikipedia entry</a> provides interesting historical information and names of some notable doodlers.  A recent feature on <a href="http://www.cbsnews.com/sunday-morning/">Sunday Morning</a> provided an entertaining and informative overview of &#8220;<a href="http://www.cbsnews.com/news/the-higher-purpose-of-doodling/">the higher purpose of doodling</a>&#8220;.</p>
<p>In addition to the meaning articulated in Wikipedia and visualized on CBS, doodles are defined in multiple ways.  <a href="http://doodle.com/">Doodle</a> can be used to schedule meetings or gatherings.  And even Google has its own version of doodles &#8211; <a href="http://www.google.com/doodles/about">Google Doodles &#8211;</a> doodles that are essentially re-drawings of the Google logo.</p>
<p>Just as there are multiple meanings of the word doodle, I consider &#8216;doodling&#8217; and &#8216;fiddling with ideas&#8217; as similar concepts. Even more so, the concepts underlying doodles and doodling include reflection, contemplation, and other forms of musings of one&#8217;s mind. An exciting part of graduate education is the lively and intense engagement with ideas. This can become apparent in and through our writings (including blogging) and our academic conversations ranging from the quiet moments of dialogue as well as to the noisy passionate debates.</p>
</div>
<div>For graduate education, academic quality is a must.  A sense of community (or communities) remains important for sharing space and place for graduate study in today&#8217;s social media rich global society.  Taking time to fiddle (to play and to play with ideas) remains critical to finding solutions to problems and preparation for the grand challenges of tomorrow.  Innovation and creativity must be at the core of scholarly inquiry and thereby keeping a &#8220;baggy idea of truth&#8221;.</div>
<div></div>
<div>All of us should find the &#8220;time to fiddle&#8221; with ideas and to engage with a &#8220;baggy idea of truth&#8221;.  Progress toward transformation and changes in higher education are dependent upon the musings of our minds especially as manifest through innovation and creativity.  What are your musings? your contemplations? your doodles?  Time to doodle.</div>


		<p>Most everyone would agree that social media has changed the ways in which we interact &#8211; not just in our social settings but in our professional lives as well.  Although somewhat slower initially to embrace social media, higher education institutions are now coming to realize the significance and the impact that social media has had upon learning.  The students are bringing social media with them and if we are to engage with them as faculty and administrators we must also engage with social media.</p>
<p>I believe that social media is fundamentally changing how we communicate within, about and outside of higher education.  The impact extends across the multiple missions of a university &#8211; teaching/learning, discovery/research, and engagement.  For this blog, I will reflect briefly on its influence upon one aspect of the graduate education, the completion and sharing of graduate student research.</p>
<p>Two of the time-honored traditions of earning a graduate degree especially a Ph.D. are the oral defense of one&#8217;s research and scholarly endeavors and the archiving of this work in a thesis and dissertation.  It is true that publications, presentations, performances and the like can be shared publicly prior to degree completion, graduate schools and faculty still honor the tradition of the thesis or dissertation as the embodiment of the body of work needed for earning a graduate degree. The archiving of the research is achieved commonly today through Electronic Thesis and Dissertation (ETD) processes, some hard bound copies placed on library shelves and in faculty offices, and frequently through journal publications or books.  The second tradition is the final oral defense.  Even though the oral defense is a public event in most cases, these defenses are usually designed for an internal and academic discipline specific audience especially the committee members. These traditions will likely continue into the near future although the venues, formats, and media will evolve.</p>
<p>With the advances in technology, the sharing (and archiving) of graduate student research is gradually changing.  Increasingly, graduate schools have adopted ETD processes and are exploring ways to increase interactivity, innovation and creativity. The open access movement has created multiple ways for graduate students to share their work and publish through <a href="http://www.plos.org/open-access/">open access</a> journals.</p>
<p>Perhaps the more significant change is the ways in which graduate student scholars communicate their research, with whom they share and the timing of such sharing.  Historically this sharing occurred at the end of the degree and primarily with the thesis/dissertation committee but the times have changed.  A few examples follow.</p>
<p>Communicating, communicating well and communicating with the public about graduate student research have become increasingly important. Toward this end, several examples come to mind: Communicating Science, Three Minute Thesis, and Dance Your Dissertation.  Previously I&#8217;ve written about <a href="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/kpdtge/index.php/2013/01/13/communicating-scholarly-endeavors/">communicating scholarly endeavors</a> including the initiative offered through the <a href="http://www.centerforcommunicatingscience.org/">Alan Alda Center for Communicating Science</a> at SUNY Stony Brook.  Similar type workshops to enhance communication about research have been and are continuing to be developed including the American Association for the Advancement of Science (<a href="http://www.aaas.org/page/communicating-science-workshops">AAAS</a>) and similar workshops through National Science Foundation (<a href="http://www.nsf.gov/events/event_summ.jsp?cntn_id=117845&amp;org=NSF">NSF</a>) and articles from National Institutes of Health (<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC3752169/">NIH</a>).  In addition, graduate schools and colleges are offering workshops or courses on communicating science such as the one we offer at Virginia Tech through the Transformative Graduate Education initiative (<a href="http://graduateschool.vt.edu/gs/tge">TGE</a>).</p>
<p><a href="http://threeminutethesis.org/docs/3MT-logo-brand-guidelines.pdf">Three Minute Thesis (3MT®)</a> was developed in 2008 by the University of Queensland. In brief, the 3MT is a research communication intended for public audiences in which the graduate students can use one &#8220;slide&#8221; and talk for three minutes only.  The 3MT competition has proven to be a very effective way for graduate students (master&#8217;s and doctoral students) to enhance one&#8217;s ability to communicate one&#8217;s research.  These competitions can now be found at the university, regional and national levels.</p>

<p>Sponsored by AAAS and Science magazine, the unique program entitled <a href="http://news.sciencemag.org/scientific-community/2013/11/dance-your-ph.d.-and-winner-…">Dance Your Dissertation</a> is another way in which graduate student research is made more accessible to the public.</p>
<div></div>
<p>Some additional musings about the use of social media in graduate student research.  The future likely includes: use of twitter hashtags (#) during defense for discussion beyond those in attendance, use of social media platforms for seeking funding for graduate student research (crowdsourcing), use of social media to connect with other conducting similar research, use of platforms (e.g., <a href="http://figshare.com/">figshare</a>) in the development of research and feedback about results, and more.  It should come as no surprise that these are already happening and more.  We must continue to embrace the principles underlying the earning of a graduate degree but understand and acknowledge that graduate student research process will continue to evolve.</p>
<p>And then there&#8217;s the discussion about originality in Kirby Ferguson&#8217;s <a href="http://everythingisaremix.info/">&#8220;Everything is a Remix&#8221;</a>.  But I&#8217;ll leave that for another time.</p>


		<p>In a quest to extend my knowledge and understanding of higher education around the world, I had the occasion to visit the National University of Singapore (<a href="http://www.nus.edu.sg/">NUS</a>) in April 2014.  A quick bus tour through the campus revealed a large, urban campus not unlike a U.S. research university filled with research facilities, academic buildings, residence halls, sport complexes, administration buildings, faculty offices and much more.</p>
<p><a href="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2014/04/NUS-logo.jpg"><img data-attachment-id="861" data-permalink="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/kpdtge/index.php/2014/04/28/national-university-of-singapore-and-utown/nus-logo/" data-orig-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2014/04/NUS-logo.jpg" data-orig-size="160,160" data-comments-opened="1" data-image-meta="{&quot;aperture&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;credit&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;camera&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;caption&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;created_timestamp&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;copyright&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;focal_length&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;iso&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;shutter_speed&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;title&quot;:&quot;&quot;}" data-image-title="NUS logo" data-image-description="" data-medium-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2014/04/NUS-logo.jpg" data-large-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2014/04/NUS-logo.jpg" class="alignleft size-thumbnail wp-image-861" alt="NUS logo" src="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2014/04/NUS-logo-150x150.jpg" width="150" height="150" srcset="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2014/04/NUS-logo-150x150.jpg 150w , https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2014/04/NUS-logo.jpg 160w " sizes="(max-width: 150px) 100vw, 150px" /></a></p>
<p>NUS began as a small medical college and in less than a century has become a large, vibrant complex for higher education in Singapore and in Asia.  A review of NUS&#8217;s <a href="http://www.nus.edu.sg/">website</a> further reveals the depth and breadth of the university and its importance to the region. Impressive!</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<p>And then we visited the newest addition to the University &#8211; <a href="http://utown.nus.edu.sg/">University Town</a> (known as UTown).  Even more impressive!  UTown is described as &#8220;an educational hub complete with residential spaces, teaching facilities and study clusters, UTown has created a lively intellectual, social and cultural environment that distinguishes the University <span class="Apple-style-span" style="font-style: normal"><span class="Apple-style-span" style="font-style: normal"><span class="Apple-style-span" style="font-style: normal"><span class="Apple-style-span" style="font-style: normal"><span class="Apple-style-span" style="font-style: normal"><span class="Apple-style-span" style="font-style: normal"><span class="Apple-style-span" style="font-style: normal"><span class="Apple-style-span" style="font-style: normal"><span class="Apple-style-span" style="font-style: normal"><span class="Apple-style-span" style="font-style: normal"><span class="Apple-style-span" style="font-style: normal"><span class="Apple-style-span" style="font-style: normal">through excellence in learning and student engagement&#8221;.</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></p>
<p><span class="Apple-style-span" style="font-family: 'Helvetica Neue', Helvetica, Arial, sans-serif"><a href="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2014/04/UTown-ERC.jpg"><img data-attachment-id="862" data-permalink="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/kpdtge/index.php/2014/04/28/national-university-of-singapore-and-utown/logo_utown/" <p><a href="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2014/04/image-about-townplaza.jpg"><img data-attachment-id="866" data-permalink="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/kpdtge/index.php/2014/04/28/national-university-of-singapore-and-utown/image-about-townplaza/"
