data-image-meta="{&quot;aperture&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;credit&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;camera&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;caption&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;created_timestamp&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;copyright&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;focal_length&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;iso&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;shutter_speed&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;title&quot;:&quot;&quot;}" data-image-title="image-about-townplaza" data-image-description="" data-medium-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2014/04/image-about-townplaza-300x199.jpg" data-large-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2014/04/image-about-townplaza.jpg" class="alignnone size-thumbnail wp-image-866" alt="image-about-townplaza" src="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2014/04/image-about-townplaza-150x150.jpg" width="150" height="150" /></a>  <span class="Apple-style-span" style="font-style: normal"><span class="Apple-style-span" style="font-style: normal"><span class="Apple-style-span" style="font-style: normal"><span class="Apple-style-span" style="font-style: normal"><span class="Apple-style-span" style="font-style: normal"><span class="Apple-style-span" style="font-style: normal"><span class="Apple-style-span" style="font-style: normal"><span class="Apple-style-span" style="font-style: normal">As we toured the complex, we were informed by the undergraduate and graduate student guides of the benefits and uniqueness of the newly developed UTown.  We visited the Educational Resources Center with its highly interactive use of space and technology followed by the Cafe and Plaza areas designed specifically to engage students (undergraduate, graduate) in conversations.  Undergraduate students and graduate students live in the multi-story and multi-building complex with the graduate students serving as mentors and resident advisors.  According to our guides, the students have a voice in the services provided and the selection of the businesses allowed in UTown; if the service is not high quality, the businesses will not be allowed to continue in UTown (very different decision-making processes here).  </span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></p>
<p>Located with UTown is the <a href="http://www.nrf.gov.sg/about-nrf/programmes/create" target="_blank">Campus for Research Excellence And Technological Enterprise (CREATE)</a>, an international research campus and innovation hub with access to students and faculty of NUS.  CREATE houses a vibrant research community with interdisciplinary research centers, technology incubators and access to Singapore corporate laboratories.</p>
<p>NUS University-Town (UTown) provided us a glimpse into higher education in the future which values innovation, sustainability and technological enhancements and actively promotes interdisciplinary educational opportunities, research experiences, and corporate and civic engagement.  Key to success seems to be its attention to and the robust efforts toward work-life integration.</p>
<p>Although smaller in scale, the efforts of the Virginia Tech Graduate School especially the Graduate Life Center (<a href="http://www.graduatelifecenter.vt.edu/">GLC</a>) and the Transformative Graduate Education (<a href="http://graduateschool.vt.edu/gs/tge">TGE</a>) initiative have followed along a similar path.  NUS UTown stands now as a model and provides excellent examples of programs and initiatives for us to consider.</p>


		<p>As is the common practice at this time of year, universities celebrate the ending of the academic year and the graduation of undergraduate and graduate students through University and Graduate Commencement ceremonies.  At the Virginia Tech <a href="http://www.unirel.vt.edu/audio_video/2014/05/051614-gradschoolcommencement.html">Graduate Commencement</a> ceremonies on May 16, 2014, the master&#8217;s and doctoral degree recipients crossed the stage to be hooded, received their diplomas and were congratulated by many.  Speeches were given, music (original composition) was played, visual reflections viewed and more.  A celebration of an ending but more importantly a new beginning.</p>
<p>To commence is to begin.  And once again, I find myself <a href="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/kpdtge/index.php/2013/05/22/on-the-eve-of-departure-once-again/">on the eve of departure</a> for the 9th year of the <a href="https://graduateschool.vt.edu/GPP">Global Perspectives Program</a> and ready to board the plane to Zurich. The 2014 theme is <em>Future of higher education: Preparing for change</em> which is not only timely for global discussions but also for personal &amp; professional reflections about change.</p>
<p><img data-attachment-id="887" data-permalink="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/kpdtge/index.php/2014/05/21/commencement-a-beginning-not-an-ending/joseph_campbell_circa_1982/" data-orig-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2014/05/Joseph_Campbell_circa_1982.jpg" data-orig-size="150,197" data-comments-opened="1" data-image-meta="{&quot;aperture&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;credit&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;camera&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;caption&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;created_timestamp&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;copyright&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;focal_length&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;iso&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;shutter_speed&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;title&quot;:&quot;&quot;}" data-image-title="Joseph_Campbell_circa_1982" data-image-description="" data-medium-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2014/05/Joseph_Campbell_circa_1982.jpg" data-large-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2014/05/Joseph_Campbell_circa_1982.jpg" class="alignright wp-image-887" style="color: inherit;font: normal normal normal 15px/normal 'Helvetica Neue', Helvetica, Arial, sans-serif;font-style: inherit;font-weight: inherit;line-height: 1.625;margin-top: 0.4em;height: 68px;background-color: #eeeeee;padding: 6px;border: 1px solid #bbbbbb" src="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2014/05/Joseph_Campbell_circa_1982-150x150.jpg" alt="Joseph_Campbell_circa_1982" width="68" height="90" /></p>
<p><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Joseph_Campbell">Joseph Campbell </a>(writer) once said &#8220;we must be willing to get rid of the life we planned so we can have the life that&#8217;s waiting for us.&#8221;  What is the life that&#8217;s waiting for us?  How shall we embrace the future?  What are the challenges and opportunities which lie ahead?  Life is a journey that continues to unfold.</p>
<p><img data-attachment-id="891" data-permalink="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/kpdtge/index.php/2014/05/21/commencement-a-beginning-not-an-ending/m_042414-president-gastanaga/" data-orig-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2014/05/M_042414-president-gastanaga.jpg" data-orig-size="240,360" data-comments-opened="1" data-image-meta="{&quot;aperture&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;credit&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;camera&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;caption&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;created_timestamp&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;copyright&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;focal_length&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;iso&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;shutter_speed&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;title&quot;:&quot;&quot;}" data-image-title="M_042414-president-gastanaga" data-image-description="" data-medium-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2014/05/M_042414-president-gastanaga-200x300.jpg" data-large-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2014/05/M_042414-president-gastanaga.jpg" class="alignleft  wp-image-891" src="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2014/05/M_042414-president-gastanaga-150x150.jpg" alt="M_042414-president-gastanaga" width="79" height="79" /></p>
<p>In the spirit of Joseph Campbell, <a href="http://www.vtnews.vt.edu/articles/2014/04/042414-president-gradspeaker.html">Claire Guthrie Gastanaga</a>, Executive Director of the American Civil Liberties Union (<a href="https://www.aclu.org/affiliate/virginia">ACLU</a>) of Virginia, in her address at the 2014 VT Graduate Commencement Ceremonies encouraged each of us to have a &#8220;courageous conscience&#8221; as we prepare for change.  She indicated that having a courageous conscience means being &#8220;a person who is actively engaged in making one&#8217;s workplace, one&#8217;s family, one&#8217;s community a better place, and a person who is not willing to compromise his or her values or integrity just to keep a job, a relationship, or a comfortable place in life&#8221;.</p>
<p>As we commence with a graduate degree from Virginia Tech or begin the journey known as GPP (Global Perspectives Program) (or both) let us experience the life that&#8217;s waiting for us!  The 2014 GPP participants will commence our collective and individual journeys at <a href="http://www.st-josef.ch/">Hotel St. Josef</a> in Zurich Switzerland at 1500 (3:00 pm) on Sunday, May 25th.</p>


		<p>For the last two weeks, the VT Global Perspectives Program (GPP) participants (@GPPVT, #gpp14) have been actively engaged in visits to selected universities in Switzerland, Italy and France. The theme for these visits was the &#8220;future of higher education: preparing for change&#8221;. Toward the end of our trip, we were joined by our GPP University of Basel colleagues at VT Center for European Studies and Architecture (CESA) in Riva San Vitale, Switzerland for a global seminar.  Lively discussions and conversations ensued.</p>
<p>Before and during the travel part of GPP, we gather information &#8211; &#8220;facts and figures&#8221;. We talk with academic administrators, faculty and students. We tour the universities &#8211; meeting rooms, buildings and &#8220;labs&#8221;.  We compare and contrast and more. We seek answers to our questions.</p>
<p>Throughout, there were some ah-ha moments, some quiet reflections and musings, shared commentary and most importantly some deep dives into the &#8220;head-spinning&#8221; information and perspectives which were shared with us. More questions and lots of them with no clear answers.  This is good because this is when we are moved out of our comfort zones and well understood contexts into a space where we become more open to hearing, seeing and perhaps understanding things differently and from another&#8217;s perspective. And this is one of the intended consequences of the Global Perspectives Program &#8211; actively seeking and gaining perspective.</p>


		<p>Many words are used to describe the changing context for higher education in the 21st century &#8211; &#8216;change&#8217; being one but more recently &#8216;disruption&#8217;, &#8216;innovation&#8217;, &#8216;global&#8217; and &#8216;learning revolution&#8217; are code for what is or should be happening in higher education.  But the use of &#8216;unruly paradox&#8217; to describe the university was new to me until recently.</p>
<p>It was actually more than a decade ago when Ben Johnson (then Chair of the Board of Trustees for Emory University) coined the term &#8220;<a href="http://news.emory.edu/stories/2013/11/er_ben_johnson_tribute/campus.html">unruly paradox</a>&#8221; in making reference to &#8220;the <a href="http://news.emory.edu/stories/2013/11/er_ben_johnson_tribute/campus.html">unrelenting swell and heave of change</a>&#8221; which is a constant in the university.  <img data-attachment-id="910" data-permalink="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/kpdtge/index.php/2014/07/11/unruly-paradox/ben_johnson520/" data-orig-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2014/07/ben_johnson520.jpg" data-orig-size="520,347" data-comments-opened="1" data-image-meta="{&quot;aperture&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;credit&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;camera&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;caption&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;created_timestamp&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;copyright&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;focal_length&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;iso&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;shutter_speed&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;title&quot;:&quot;&quot;}" data-image-title="ben_johnson520" data-image-description="" data-medium-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2014/07/ben_johnson520-300x200.jpg" data-large-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2014/07/ben_johnson520.jpg" class="alignleft size-thumbnail wp-image-910" src="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2014/07/ben_johnson520-150x150.jpg" alt="ben_johnson520" width="150" height="150" />In 2000, Johnson articulated that &#8220;<em>a great university is a thing of unruly paradox.  It is a place of tranquil reflection and a testing place and indeed a battleground of outrageous ideas&#8221;  </em>He continued that a university<em> &#8220;requires stability, yet is a catalyst for change. It teaches respect for boundaries, yet encourages pushing those boundaries. It is a place of self-conscious egalitarianism, yet a place of studied rank. It trains for the sacred, as well as the secular. It gleans from the past, to prepare for the future…&#8221;</em></p>
<p>How do we prepare for the future?  What will the universities look like in 10 years? How have they changed in the past 5 years?  As an &#8216;unruly paradox&#8217;, the university must manage the tensions of history, boundaries, and stability and the pushing of limits, outrageous ideas and being a catalyst for change.</p>
<p>Higher education more broadly defined must also accept the responsibility for change and adaptation. The pace of change is astonishing and doesn&#8217;t appear to be slowing down?  For example, when the class of 2014 entered in 2010, the iPad had been only recently introduced and four years later (for their commencement) there are four generations of iPads plus the iPad Air. The impact of technology and social media is significant not only in the &#8220;classroom&#8221; but throughout the university.</p>
<p>In addition to technology, there are many other factors that could be discussed here.  A critical factor I wish to highlight here revolves around access: access to higher education especially defining the student/learner, discussion of delivery modes and locations for learning as well as access to education, research, knowledge through open educational resources and open access publishing.</p>
<p>Other factors are brought to light by Drew Faust, President of Harvard University. In a <a href="http://www.bloomberg.com/video/harvard-s-faust-on-financing-higher-education-6uRjoIghSKCKLWliCnx4Gw.html">recent interview</a> with <a href="http://www.bloomberg.com/video/harvard-s-faust-on-financing-higher-education-6uRjoIghSKCKLWliCnx4Gw.html">Bloomberg </a>about the future and financing of higher education, she identified three major forces facing higher education: digital revolution especially its impact on learning and teaching, global context and expectations, and the breaking down of traditional disciplines boundaries. She briefly discussed MOOCs and other digital learning modalities as helping us understand more about learning and processing of information and that they must inform our teaching. Embracing a global perspective has become even more important and defining the global context and expectations more challenging. Yes, disciplines are evolving and transcending boundaries (albeit somewhat slowly at times) and moving toward the interfaces between and among disciplines and into interdisciplinary and transdisciplinary programs.</p>
<p>Change is challenge and a condition of an &#8216;unruly paradox&#8217;. Ultimately, change will impact the organizational structure as well as the functionality of higher education. Change is also an opportunity. One of our opportunities in preparing for the future is to embrace innovation and creativity within higher education and prepare not only ourselves but especially the students/learners to develop the &#8220;habits of mind to adapt to change&#8221;.  These habits will last a lifetime.</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>


		<p>The Virginia Tech Graduate School is leading an initiative to join the conversation with Connected Courses this semester.  Our efforts will include graduate students and faculty in the conversation.  Pleased to participate.</p>


		<p>This semester many of us are engaged with a national initiative entitled <a href="http://connectedcourses.net/">Connected</a> Courses. At Virginia Tech, we developed our own <a href="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/activecolearning/connected-courses/">active co-learning</a> group of faculty and graduate students to interface with each other and <a href="http://connectedcourses.net/">ConnectedCourses</a>. And the dialogue ensued.</p>
<p>The first unit (of six) challenged us to engage with &#8220;why we need a why&#8221; and participants were asked to reflect specifically on &#8220;why we teach&#8221;.   Michael <a href="http://http://www.k-state.edu/media/mediaguide/bios/weschbio.html">Wesch</a> and his colleagues (Randy Bass, Cathy Davidson) shared some of their teaching experiences, introduced purpose driven not just content driven courses, and challenged us to contemplate &#8220;<a href="http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=f_mcTLlG_wg">why we need a why</a>&#8220;.  They asked us to share &#8220;why I teach&#8221;.</p>
<p>As I pondered &#8220;why I teach&#8221;, my thoughts quickly moved toward questions of &#8220;why&#8221; beyond teaching as related to the future of higher education and the future professoriate. We are often taught about the 7 questions &#8211; the who, what, when, where, how, why and why not. As one who is focused more on process, I especially appreciate the why, the how and the why not.</p>
<p>In the graduate course I teach, GRAD 5104 Preparing the Future Professoriate, we have been discussing the roles and responsibilities of faculty (teaching, research, service and more) and the changing nature of higher education. As the students consider their future as faculty, they must ask themselves which university they will choose and why, the roles and responsibilities they will embrace and why, the pedagogical strategies to utilize and why, the possibilities for change and why not, and how they will engage with colleagues, students, and community.  Inasmuch as the path forward for higher education requires dynamic mostly non-linear processes and will offer some exciting opportunities, the faculty and the future faculty must innovate, create, and lead change.  The questions they ask and answer especially the &#8220;why&#8221; will inform the future.</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>


		<p>Building academic community for graduate students has been a goal of the Virginia Tech Graduate School since at least 2002.  Universities traditionally have fostered a sense of community for the undergraduate experience but often left the graduate student experience in the hands of the department, the &#8220;lab&#8221;/team, or the faculty member.  In my role as Vice President and Dean for Graduate Education, I have encouraged the VT Graduate School to embrace community as an integral part of graduate education.  In 2003, we developed an initiative called Transformative Graduate Education (<a href="http://graduateschool.vt.edu/gs/tge">TGE</a>) with a focus on preparing the<a href="http://graduateschool.vt.edu/gs/tge/fp"> future professoriate</a> (PFP).  Since then the VT Graduate School has continued to expand and enhance the graduate student experience through building academic community especially through the <a href="http://www.graduatelifecenter.vt.edu/">Graduate Life Center</a> which provides a physical &#8220;space and place&#8221; for graduate education. Now, that space and place for graduate education must naturally go beyond physical space to the digital world.</p>
<p>As the connectivity and interactivity of the web expands, so must the initiatives and opportunities for higher education to share community, networks and connections.  The <a href="http://connectedcourses.net/">Connected Courses</a> initiative provides the platform for a &#8220;collaborative community of faculty&#8221; to explore networked learning, open access and the values of the &#8220;open web&#8221;.  We determined that Connected Courses could also provide a wonderful opportunity to bring the faculty and the future faculty together in conversation about <a href="http://blogs.lt.vt.edu/activecolearning/">active co-learning at VT.</a> To expand our efforts in transforming graduate education, graduate students must be in the conversation about embracing change and to foster <a href="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/kpdtge/index.php/2013/11/10/futurisktic/">futurisktic </a>thinking (<a href="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/kpdtge/index.php/2013/03/09/graduate-education-and-web-2-0/">web 2.0</a>, <a href="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/kpdtge/index.php/2014/03/03/graduate-education-and-social-media/">social media</a>).</p>
<p>The dialogue begins.  Please join us.</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>


		<p>In my role as Vice President and Dean for Graduate School at Virginia Tech, I have thought a lot about transforming graduate education in general and more specifically about preparation in an academic area as well as preparation for career(s) after degree completion.  The graduate dean should think about these things and create opportunities and programs for graduate students to enhance their preparation for success in the career options they can pursue following degree completion. Academic area mentioned above does not mean simply discipline or department but rather encompassing departments, programs and includes multi-, inter- and transdisciplinary areas of study.  And careers refer to academic and careers outside academe and the fact that graduates should expect a lifetime of career changes.</p>
<p>Many of us in higher education often make reference to the &#8220;I&#8221; educated or the &#8220;T&#8221; educated individuals. The &#8220;I&#8221; has been used to refer to depth in the discipline and the &#8220;T&#8221; offering breadth beyond the depth within the discipline. Breadth can be understood in terms of going beyond one&#8217;s discipline moving toward multi or interdisciplinary thinking.  Breadth can also be interpreted as moving toward a more holistic education that of a well rounded person.  Although applicable to graduate education, this breadth has been associated more commonly within the undergraduate experience; education in a discipline plus educational opportunities beyond one&#8217;s major(s).</p>
<p>I have reflected on the &#8220;I&#8221; and the &#8220;T&#8221; in the context of and advocating for interdisciplinary graduate education.  In a <a href="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/kpdtge/index.php/2012/03/28/interdisciplinary-thinking-pi-and-adaptive-innovators/">blog </a>post entitled &#8220;interdisciplinary thinking, Pi and adaptive innovators&#8221;, I introduced the Pi symbol as a visual representation of interdisciplinary thinking and adaptive innovators.</p>
<p><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi"><img data-attachment-id="73" data-permalink="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/kpdtge/index.php/2012/03/28/interdisciplinary-thinking-pi-and-adaptive-innovators/images-2/" data-orig-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2012/03/images1.jpg" data-orig-size="225,225" data-comments-opened="1" data-image-meta="{&quot;aperture&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;credit&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;camera&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;caption&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;created_timestamp&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;copyright&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;focal_length&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;iso&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;shutter_speed&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;title&quot;:&quot;&quot;}" data-image-title="images" data-image-description="" data-medium-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2012/03/images1.jpg" data-large-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2012/03/images1.jpg" class="alignnone  wp-image-73" src="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2012/03/images1-150x150.jpg" alt="" width="34" height="34" srcset="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2012/03/images1-150x150.jpg 150w , https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2012/03/images1.jpg 225w " sizes="(max-width: 34px) 100vw, 34px" /></a></p>
<p><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi">Pi </a>is primarily understood as a mathematical constant or a Greek letter.  Much has been written about Pi in those contexts but I use it here as a symbol of and metaphor for interdisciplinarity.   As shown above, the symbol Pi includes three lines: two vertical lines and one line across the top of the two vertical lines.  Beyond the straight line, each of these lines has an additional feature at one end.</p>
<p>I find the visual compelling in its simplicity.  Interdisciplinary thinking and education requires depth in one of more disciplines of study and the ability to integrate across the disciplines.  There must be a firm foundation (wider base) grounded in the knowledge within a discipline and a strong connection (anchor, hook) into the academic field(s) of study.  The horizontal line provides the link between the academic pillars.  Specifically, this line represents the link that facilitates meaningful connections between (among) the academic areas of study, integrates knowledge and understanding across the disciplines and extends beyond the pillars of the disciplines to situate knowledge and understanding in the societal context.</p>
<p>Societal context is important.  Academic leaders need to acknowledge and confirm the underlying principle and purpose of higher education &#8220;to educate&#8221; but we must also be mindful of the need to implement programs that incorporate the knowledge, skills and abilities for success in the work place. Reports from the Council of Graduate Schools (CGS) support and encourage <a href="https://www.cgsnet.org/university-leaders-issue-statement-interdisciplinarity-graduate-education-and-research">interdisciplinary graduate education. </a>Two additional reports from Educational Testing Services (ETS) and Council of Graduate Schools (CGS)  articulate clearly the responsibility of Graduate Schools to prepare graduates for the professoriate and careers outside academe.<a href="http://www.fgereport.org/"><img data-attachment-id="1003" data-permalink="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/kpdtge/index.php/2014/10/27/i-t-and-pi-as-metaphors-for-graduate-education/report/" data-orig-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2014/10/report.png" data-orig-size="168,216" data-comments-opened="1" data-image-meta="{&quot;aperture&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;credit&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;camera&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;caption&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;created_timestamp&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;copyright&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;focal_length&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;iso&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;shutter_speed&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;title&quot;:&quot;&quot;}" data-image-title="report" data-image-description="" data-medium-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2014/10/report.png" data-large-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2014/10/report.png" class="alignleft size-thumbnail wp-image-1003" src="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2014/10/report-150x150.png" alt="report" width="150" height="150" /></a> <a href="http://pathwaysreport.org/"><img data-attachment-id="1004" data-permalink="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/kpdtge/index.php/2014/10/27/i-t-and-pi-as-metaphors-for-graduate-education/pathreportcover/" data-orig-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2014/10/pathreportcover.jpg" data-orig-size="181,234" data-comments-opened="1" data-image-meta="{&quot;aperture&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;credit&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;camera&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;caption&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;created_timestamp&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;copyright&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;focal_length&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;iso&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;shutter_speed&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;title&quot;:&quot;&quot;}" data-image-title="pathreportcover" data-image-description="" data-medium-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2014/10/pathreportcover.jpg" data-large-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2014/10/pathreportcover.jpg" class="alignleft size-thumbnail wp-image-1004" src="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2014/10/pathreportcover-150x150.jpg" alt="pathreportcover" width="150" height="150" /></a></p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<p>In our ongoing efforts to transform graduate education (and higher education), academic leaders should continue to support &#8220;I&#8221; and &#8220;T&#8221; education and we must definitely encourage more &#8220;Pi&#8221;-educated individuals.  The universities for the 21st century need scholars who have the depth, breadth, and integrated interdisciplinary perspectives to address the complex problems facing us in 21st century.</p>


		<p>Blogging has a role in 21st century higher education including graduate education.</p>
<p>Although blogging and blogs have been around for more years, Gardner Campbell (currently <a href="http://www.provost.vcu.edu/about/office-leadership/vice-provost-for-learning-innovation-and-student-success/">Vice Provost for Learning Innovation and Student Success</a> at Virginia Commonwealth University) was on the Virginia Tech faculty and passionately advocated for the use of blogs for the undergraduate students in the VT Residential Honor&#8217;s College.<img class="alignleft" src="http://campustechnology.com/articles/2011/08/10/%7E/media/EDU/CampusTechnology/Images/2011/08/Gardner%20Campbell.ashx" alt="" width="92" height="112" /> In August 2011, he wrote that blogging could &#8220;<a href="http://campustechnology.com/articles/2011/08/10/how-blogging-can-catalyze-learning.aspx">catalyze learning</a>&#8221; through the framework of &#8220;narrate, curate, and share&#8221;.  His efforts inspired me as Vice President and Dean for Graduate Education to establish my own blog and to incorporate blogging in Graduate School administrative activities and more importantly as an integral part of the Transformative Graduate Education (<a href="http://graduateschool.vt.edu/gs/tge">TGE</a>) initiative. (Note: currently we are also engaged in other types of social media [e.g. <a href="https://www.facebook.com/VTGraduateSchool">Facebook</a>, <a href="https://twitter.com/vtgradcommunity">Twitter</a>, <a href="https://www.linkedin.com/edu/school?id=162003&amp;trk=edu-up-nav-menu-home">LInkedIn</a>] but the focus here is blogging).</p>
<p>For the past several years, <img data-attachment-id="1014" data-permalink="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/kpdtge/index.php/2014/12/17/blogging-in-graduate-education/blogging-as-pedagogy2/" data-orig-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2014/12/blogging-as-pedagogy2.jpg" data-orig-size="1024,768" data-comments-opened="1" data-image-meta="{&quot;aperture&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;credit&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;camera&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;caption&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;created_timestamp&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;copyright&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;focal_length&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;iso&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;shutter_speed&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;title&quot;:&quot;&quot;}" data-image-title="blogging-as-pedagogy2" data-image-description="" data-medium-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2014/12/blogging-as-pedagogy2-300x225.jpg" data-large-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2014/12/blogging-as-pedagogy2.jpg" class="alignleft  wp-image-1014" src="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2014/12/blogging-as-pedagogy2-150x150.jpg" alt="blogging-as-pedagogy2" width="173" height="173" />blogging has become an integral part of several graduate courses offered within the Graduate School&#8217;s Transformative Graduate Education (TGE) initiative including GRAD 5104 <a href="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/grad5104/">Preparing the Future Professoriate</a>, GRAD 5114 Contemporary Pedagogy (<a href="https://tlos.vt.edu/gedi/">GEDI</a>), GRAD 5214 <a href="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/diversity/">Diversity and Inclusion in a Global Society</a> and GRAD 5014 <a href="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/grad5014/">Ethics and Scholarly Integrity</a>.  Embracing &#8220;blogging as pedagogy&#8221; in these courses, graduate students are encouraged to &#8220;reflect, read, write and share&#8221; with their colleagues on topics related to not only preparing the future professoriate but current issues facing higher education.  Although some expressed initial hesitancy to blog and were new to blogging, most of the graduate students became actively engaged with blogging.  Many commented that they enjoyed learning a new skill and found it valuable.  Indeed, blogging is an important aspect of modern learning.</p>
<p>Beyond its use in graduate courses, blogging was introduced to the faculty and graduate students associated with interdisciplinary graduate education at VT specifically through the Interdisciplinary Graduate Education program (<a href="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/vtige/igep-program-overview/">IGEPs)</a>.  Each IGEP has created a blog that feeds into the &#8220;<a href="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/vtige/">mother blog</a>&#8221; hosted on the website which provides the shared space for cross IGEP discussion.  Another example is the use of blogs with the <a href="http://graduateschool.vt.edu/GPP">Global Perspectives Program</a> organized through the VT Graduate School.  The &#8220;grandmother <a href="http://blogs.lt.vt.edu/globalperspectives/">blog</a>&#8221; for the Global Perspectives program gathers and shares blog posts from several &#8220;mother blogs&#8221; including GPP <a href="http://blogs.lt.vt.edu/pfpswitzerland/">Switzerland</a>, GPP <a href="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/pfpchile/">Chile</a>, GPP <a href="http://blogs.lt.vt.edu/gppalumni/">alumni</a>, and from the <a href="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/gpgraddeans/">graduate deans</a> experience.  This provides but one example of how individual blogs can feed into a common space for collective reflection on global perspectives and engage colleagues around the world.</p>
<p>Blogs are but one form of communication in today&#8217;s academic world.  In sharing and disseminating our scholarship, we have typically used publications (articles, books, exhibits and more) and presentations. Technology used in our connected world allows for other ways of sharing today.  Blogs use a different voice and often reach different audiences in different ways but represent a valuable tool for scholars and practitioners.  Graduate students as the future faculty and career professionals need to gain these skills as part of their graduate degree and preparation for the 21st century workforce.</p>


		<p>Diversity and multicultural were two of the early terms that were used to describe efforts toward increasing demographic diversity in the corporate world and higher education dating back to the 1980s.  Since then, the terminology has continued to evolve as have the efforts and activities.</p>
<p>Many of the initial efforts were focused on access to higher education by individuals of diverse backgrounds especially individuals of color.  Recruitment became a priority with less attention paid to retention and graduation although these would follow. These early efforts and initiatives were based mostly on compositional diversity.  Studies were done, data collected and analyzed. Recommendations made and strategies put forward which became common practices at colleges and universities around the United States. Chief Diversity Officers (CDO) and associated offices were established and charged to increase diversity, including efforts for faculty, staff and students.</p>
<p>In developing successful retention strategies and degree completion among students of diverse backgrounds, universities were forced to examine the university environment and how the climate (perhaps &#8216;chilly climate&#8217;) influenced not only access but more so retention, and ultimately graduation. As a result, support programs and student success offices evolved. And the language and terminology continued to evolve.</p>
<p>The terms of &#8220;inclusion&#8221; and &#8220;inclusiveness&#8221; were made prominent by the American Association of Colleges and Universities (AACU) <a href="http://www.aacu.org/programs-partnerships/making-excellence-inclusive">Inclusive Excellence</a> initiative.  Many universities would ultimately adopt this approach and the &#8220;inclusion&#8221; terminology now leads many offices and initiatives.</p>
<p>Virginia Tech adopted the AACU inclusive excellence approach which guided our most recent diversity strategic plan and led the way to our current initiative.  Since early fall 2014, Virginia Tech has embarked on a journey toward inclusivity through our &#8220;<a href="http://www.inclusive.vt.edu/">InclusiveVT</a>&#8221; initiative.  In developing the university-wide initiative, VT President Sands indicated that inclusion comes first and with inclusion diversity will follow. Through InclusiveVT, senior administrators were charged with the responsibility to propose and implement specific <a href="http://www.inclusive.vt.edu/initiatives/index.html">initiatives</a> designed to foster inclusivity throughout the university.  These efforts are focused on people, programs, and policies and are identified with the four aspects of inclusive excellence: access and success, campus climate and interior up relations, education and scholarship, and institutional infrastructure. In an attempt to increase communication and university engagement with the initiatives, activities and resources are posted on the <a href="http://www.inclusive.vt.edu/">website</a>, information shared via twitter (@inclusiveVT) and through our blog entitled <a href="http://blogs.lt.vt.edu/diversity/">diversity dialogue</a>.</p>
<p>As indicated above, there are many activities and efforts underway at Virginia Tech which can move us toward becoming &#8220;inclusiveVT&#8221;.  But the efforts and conversation must be pushed further.  If we are to become truly inclusive I believe that we must embrace inclusion as a learning community &#8211; not necessarily a Learning Community in the more formal sense but a learning community in which we (faculty, staff, students) work together with shared goals and aspirations.  In this learning community we will need to work together and collaboratively.  Not as top down or bottom up but as fellow travelers on this journey.  In this learning community, we can and must learn from each other regardless of one&#8217;s position or status.  We must be open to hearing the voices of others who are different from us and sharing openly.  As a community, we will need to understand &#8220;<a href="http://amptoons.com/blog/files/mcintosh.html">white privilege</a>&#8220;, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Microaggression_theory">micro aggressions</a>, and <a href="http://www.tolerance.org/activity/test-yourself-hidden-bias">unconscious bias</a> and engage in efforts to create affirming environments for all.  We should feel empowered to speak and required to listen.  In this learning community, we can and will learn.</p>
<p>The journey has begun.  Please join us and offer your reflections along the way.</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>


		<p>Words like revolution, transformation and risk-taking are not necessarily common in higher education&#8230;.until recently.  And I&#8217;m delighted to see the change.</p>
<p>&#8220;Of the i<a href="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2015/03/Use-of-the-univ.jpg"><img data-attachment-id="1082" data-permalink="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/kpdtge/index.php/2015/04/09/risk-taking-and-higher-education-not-an-oxymoron/use-of-the-univ/" data-orig-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2015/03/Use-of-the-univ.jpg" data-orig-size="106,160" data-comments-opened="1" data-image-meta="{&quot;aperture&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;credit&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;camera&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;caption&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;created_timestamp&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;copyright&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;focal_length&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;iso&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;shutter_speed&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;title&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;orientation&quot;:&quot;0&quot;}" data-image-title="Use of the univ" data-image-description="" data-medium-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2015/03/Use-of-the-univ.jpg" data-large-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2015/03/Use-of-the-univ.jpg" class="alignleft size-thumbnail wp-image-1082" src="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2015/03/Use-of-the-univ-106x150.jpg" alt="Use of the univ" width="106" height="150" /></a>nstitutions that had been established in the Western world by 1520, 85 still exist – Catholic Church, the Parliament of the Isle of Man, of Iceland and of Great Britain, several Swiss cantons, and 70 universities. Of these, perhaps the universities have experienced the least change.&#8221; (Kerr 2001, p.115 from <em>The Uses of the University</em> originally published in 1982 by Harvard University press).</p>
<p>As indicated above and common knowledge, universities have been slow to change throughout much of history at least through the 20th century.  But the advancements in technology of the 21st century have definitely precipitated change in almost all aspects of the university.  We have seen changes in the teaching and learning mission.  Research and discovery have expanded due to the use of technology as have the ways in which we disseminate scholarship.  Innovation and entrepreneurship have become common in today&#8217;s universities.  And of course, administrative processes and communication strategies sometime bear little resemblance to the past.</p>
<p>Although change is occurring, more is needed.  Many books have been written, op-ed pieces published and reports issued about the status of higher education today.  Once such report entitled &#8220;<a href="http://www.ippr.org/publications/an-avalanche-is-coming-higher-education-and-the-revolution-ahead">An Avalanche is Coming</a>&#8221; offered the following:<a href="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2015/03/pub-avalanche-130305_10432.693d2106.jpg"><img data-attachment-id="1078" data-permalink="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/kpdtge/index.php/2015/04/09/risk-taking-and-higher-education-not-an-oxymoron/pub-avalanche-130305_10432-693d2106/" data-orig-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2015/03/pub-avalanche-130305_10432.693d2106.jpg" data-orig-size="158,224" data-comments-opened="1" data-image-meta="{&quot;aperture&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;credit&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;camera&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;caption&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;created_timestamp&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;copyright&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;focal_length&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;iso&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;shutter_speed&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;title&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;orientation&quot;:&quot;1&quot;}" data-image-title="pub-avalanche-130305_10432.693d2106" data-image-description="" data-medium-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2015/03/pub-avalanche-130305_10432.693d2106.jpg" data-large-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2015/03/pub-avalanche-130305_10432.693d2106.jpg" class="alignright size-thumbnail wp-image-1078" src="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2015/03/pub-avalanche-130305_10432.693d2106-150x150.jpg" alt="pub-avalanche-130305_10432.693d2106" width="150" height="150" /></a></p>
<p>&#8220;Our belief is that deep, radical and urgent transformation is required in higher education as much as it is in the school systems.  Our fear is that, perhaps as a result of complacency, caution or anxiety, or a combination of all three, the pace of change is too slow and the nature of change too incremental&#8221; (March 2013, p. 3)</p>
<p>If the &#8220;pace of change too slow and nature too incremental&#8221;, the leadership for the 21st century universities must be willing to challenge the status quo and take risks.   We must be &#8220;<a href="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/kpdtge/?s=futurisktic">futurisktic</a>&#8220;.  In that blog (2013), I wrote:</p>
<p>&#8220;Being futurisktic is about change.  It is about embracing risk as an integral aspect of      change.  Risk should not be viewed as a negative but risk taking will likely force us out of our comfort zones.  By doing so, it allows us to acknowledge and embrace the meaningfulness and value of change&#8230;. Being futurisktic is about pushing ourselves and pushing the limits as is so wonderfully exemplified in the video entitled <a href="http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=rDiUVS_-4_Q">the future is ours</a>.&#8221;</p>
<p>A recent example of pushing oneself and stretching beyond one&#8217;s comfort zone is the article about &#8220;<a href="https://www.google.com/url?sa=t&amp;rct=j&amp;q=&amp;esrc=s&amp;source=web&amp;cd=1&amp;ved=0CB8QFjAA&amp;url=https%3A%2F%2Fsty.presswarehouse.com%2Fsites%2Fstylus%2Fresrcs%2Fchapters%2F1579229743_otherchap.pdf&amp;ei=DLomVbubKoucsAW3y4CwCw&amp;usg=AFQjCNHk4nBQvpom11y51jrP_Mn5hrGjGw&amp;bvm=bv.90491159,d.b2w&amp;cad=rja">From safe spaces to brave spaces</a>&#8221; by Brian Arao and Kristi Clemens (2013).  The discussion of moving from &#8220;safe&#8221; to &#8220;brave&#8221; spaces has been especially pertinent to social justice and diversity. Several universities (e.g., UMBC, University of Michigan, Berkeley, UCLA, NYU) have initiated programs, dialogue and issued guidelines.  The metaphor of safe to brave should be embraced by university communities and utilized in considering futurisktic ideas and embracing change and institutional transformation.</p>
<p>I realize that change is difficult for some and especially in higher education where the culture tends to reinforce caution and the status quo.  As stated in &#8220;An Avalanche is Coming&#8221;, higher education needs transformation and individual leaders who can help lead the way.  &#8220;Ethical Ambition: Living a Life of Meaning and Worth&#8221; by Derrick Bell (2002) provides some guidance for these leaders toward this end.  He offers advise for being successful and maintaining a sense of integrity.  His message is simple in that he &#8220;urges us to liv<a href="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2015/04/Ethical-Ambition.jpg"><img data-attachment-id="1094" data-permalink="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/kpdtge/index.php/2015/04/09/risk-taking-and-higher-education-not-an-oxymoron/ethical-ambition/" data-orig-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2015/04/Ethical-Ambition.jpg" data-orig-size="104,160" data-comments-opened="1" data-image-meta="{&quot;aperture&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;credit&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;camera&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;caption&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;created_timestamp&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;copyright&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;focal_length&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;iso&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;shutter_speed&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;title&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;orientation&quot;:&quot;0&quot;}" data-image-title="Ethical Ambition" data-image-description="" data-medium-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2015/04/Ethical-Ambition.jpg" data-large-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2015/04/Ethical-Ambition.jpg" class="alignleft size-thumbnail wp-image-1094" src="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2015/04/Ethical-Ambition-104x150.jpg" alt="Ethical Ambition" width="104" height="150" /></a>e a life of passion, to have the courage to take risks for what we believe in, to rely on our loved ones and out faith for support during hard times, and to have the humility to know when our best intentions go awry&#8221; (front cover).</p>


		<p>The &#8220;eve of departure&#8221; for the 2015 Global Perspectives Program is rapidly approaching.  And as I contemplate another departure, I&#8217;m struck by the fact that this is the 10th year of the program.   For me, each year brings a similar level of excitement and wonder about the journey we will take individually and collectively.  Every cohort is different although each cohort believes they are the &#8220;best&#8221;.   Better or best are not words I use to describe the various cohorts for each cohort is unique and brings their perspectives to the journey.  Our individual goals and objectives vary; our interactions evolve, a sense of community builds and the experience lasts much longer than our time abroad.  Each trip is terrific producing fond memories and friendships that last a life-time.  To date, more than 120 VT graduate students participated in the GPP program &#8211; future professoriate: <a href="http://graduateschool.vt.edu/GPP">global perspectives program.</a>  And I have appreciated all of the experiences over the years.</p>
<p>So on the eve of my departure this year, I reflect on the 10 years of the program.   I looked back on two previous blogs about departures for <a href="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/kpdtge/index.php/2012/05/14/on-the-eve-of-departure/">GPP 2012</a> and for<a href="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/kpdtge/?s=eve+of+departure"> GPP&#8217;13</a>.   In both of these blogs, a theme emerges about observation and reflection.  I quote:  &#8220;As a part of the experience, I ask the Virginia Tech participants to keep journals and to write about their observations and personal reflections.  As I challenge myself daily, I encourage the GPPers to see new things and to see things in new ways (e.g., the <a href="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/kpdtge/index.php/2012/05/14/on-the-eve-of-departure/">doors and windows</a>) and to see the <a title="Seeing the unobvious: Fed Ex and other visuals" href="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/kpdtge/index.php/2012/10/24/seeing-the-unobvious-fed-ex-and-other-visuals/">unobvious</a>.&#8221;  When we meet at &#8220;Hotel St. Josef in Zurich, Switzerland at 3pm (1500) on Sunday May 24, 2015&#8221; I will once again talk about looking at the windows and doors, markets and stores, streets and sidewalks, and more.  And I will encourage us to look for the unobvious and see things through a different cultural lens.</p>
<p>The eve of departure always tends to coincide with VT Commencement Ceremonies.  This seems fitting inasmuch as <a href="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/kpdtge/index.php/2014/05/21/commencement-a-beginning-not-an-ending/">commencement is also a beginning</a> &#8211; a beginning of new journey.  GPP&#8217;15 is a beginning, a new journey and one that we anticipate.</p>
<p>The fourteen plus Michael and me will travel different routes to Zurich, spend the next 12 days together and on June 4th we begin our return and re-entry.  Please read follow the <a href="http://blogs.lt.vt.edu/pfpswitzerland/">GPP Switzerland blog</a>, read about the <a href="http://blogs.lt.vt.edu/pfpswitzerland/2015-gpp-cohort/">VT participants</a> and UniBasel group along the way, and follow up on social media (<a href="https://www.facebook.com/GlobalPerspectivesProgramVirginiaTech">Facebook</a>, twitter @gppvt or @kpdepauw, and more).  Please check out #gppswiss15.</p>
<p>A new way to follow our trip and join in the fun &#8211; http://tripvis.org.  Stay tuned, there&#8217;s more to come.</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>


		<div>A significant portion of the 2015 Global Perspectives Program has come to a close and yet the impact has not been fully realized.</div>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<div>Departing the Villa Maderni in Riva San Vitale Switzerland, the Virginia Tech participants head home and await the arrival of the University of Basel participants to Blacksburg and the culminating event at the Swiss Embassy in Washington DC.  And of course, we look forward to these next events and further establishing connections with those who have become colleagues.</div>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<div>We exit individually to re-enter our professional and personal lives but we will always be a part of this group &#8211; GPP2015.  During the days of GPP15, information was gathered and analyzed.  Facts learned and recorded.  Photos taken and posted.  Tweets sent and blogs shared.  Friendships formed. Future collaborations and collegial relationships considered.  As academics and future faculty, we value these and appreciate the opportunity to do so.</div>
<div></div>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<div>The Future Professoriate: Global Perspectives Program is much more than the professional experiences and knowledge gained.  It is also about intrapersonal and interpersonal growth, introspection and reflection.  As I reflected on the experiences in the last ten years and especially this year, I kept thinking about vulnerability and the GPP moments that we experienced during the time we spent together.   Not only does the program challenge us to learn to live with strangers, get out of our comfort zones, to face our biases and privilege openly and honesty and to deal with conflict and tensions but also provides a &#8220;safe&#8221; space in which these can occur.  I observed this year that the &#8220;safe&#8221; space often evolved into a &#8220;brave&#8221; space which was safe and allowed for ideas, beliefs and hypotheses to be explored, critiqued and more fully developed. From those moments of vulnerability I could see the exploration and emergence of an authentic self.  Inasmuch as authenticity is a valued characteristic of an effective and engaging faculty member, I&#8217;m pleased that the GPP experience can create a space for learning more about oneself and allowing oneself to be open to change.</div>
<p>&nbsp;</p>


		<p>My morning walk between the Villa Moderni and my apartment revealed the morning rituals of a small town in Ticino region of Switzerland &#8211; Riva San Vitale.  Although it is likely that these customs and morning activities are common in other towns I could feel the essence of this town awakening to a new day.  The street cleaner using straw brooms to clear the small trash from the day before.  The postal worker delivering the mail and newspapers to the homes along the alley.  Greetings offered to everyone who passed by including me, a familiar stranger in this town.  The butcher receiving the meat delivery and other cold goods.  The bread and cheese already delivered to be consumed with morning coffee.  Stores and restaurants opening. The rain has stopped and the fog is lifting to what will be another &#8220;beautiful day in paradise.&#8221;</p>
<p>The day of departure for GPP15 has come and gone and in fact, we&#8217;re close to the end of this phase of the global perspectives program.  Over the past 10+ days, we have experienced the awakening of new days, not just with the rituals of the morning but also of new revelations, insights and questions to explore.  Each day we have learned some things new (and anew), passionately discussed issues and examined our own biases and perspectives.  We have continuously reflected on that has been shared with us through our visits to the universities. We have, I hope, learned to see things differently and understand through a different lens.</p>
<p>Today the UniBasel participants arrive in Ticino and the GPP15 experience will expand.  A rich discussion of &#8220;global higher education: accountability and relevance&#8221; is expected along with lively more social conversation in the garden.  And then onto the Swiss Embassy in Washington DC.</p>
<p>The GPP15 experience began some days ago and it will continue.  GPP provides not only a challenge but also opportunities to effect change in global higher education.  Please join me in finding ways to continue the journey.</p>
<p>I will miss the morning walks to the Villa.</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>


		<p>Virginia Tech has developed an initiative called <a href="http://www.inclusive.vt.edu" target="_blank">InclusiveVT</a> which now serves an umbrella for inclusion and diversity efforts.  VT President Timothy Sands has publicly stated that inclusion is the goal and with inclusion will come diversity.  For more information, see the <a href="http://www.inclusive.vt.edu">InclusiveVT </a>website and <a href="http://blogs.lt.vt.edu/diversity/" target="_blank">blog</a>.</p>
<p>Most of us would say that we understand and would be able to define inclusion. And we would commonly define inclusion as the &#8220;action of including or being included within a group or structure&#8221;.  Although accurate, inclusion is and has to be more.</p>
<p>From my perspective, inclusion thus is not simply about including individuals of diverse backgrounds into existing groups or structures (society).  It has to be about changing the culture of the group or structure (institution) as well so that individuals could be and are included.   Without such change, individuals might be &#8220;present&#8221; in the structure but might and often do feel excluded.  With cultural change, the modified structure would then be more inclusive and less representative of the status quo. Institutions of higher education have been very slow to change but they must.  To do less is to fall short of the transformational change toward inclusivity needed in higher education.</p>


		<p><a href="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2015/08/Klein-book.jpg"><img data-attachment-id="1161" data-permalink="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/kpdtge/index.php/2015/08/06/interdisciplinary-thinking/klein-book/" data-orig-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2015/08/Klein-book.jpg" data-orig-size="128,191" data-comments-opened="1" data-image-meta="{&quot;aperture&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;credit&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;camera&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;caption&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;created_timestamp&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;copyright&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;focal_length&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;iso&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;shutter_speed&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;title&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;orientation&quot;:&quot;0&quot;}" data-image-title="Klein book" data-image-description="" data-medium-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2015/08/Klein-book.jpg" data-large-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2015/08/Klein-book.jpg" class="alignleft size-full wp-image-1161" src="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2015/08/Klein-book.jpg" alt="Klein book" width="128" height="191" /></a>The higher education community has long discussed, debated and defined (and redefined) interdisciplinary education and research.  Published in 1990, Julie Thompson Klein wrote the first comprehensive overview of <em>interdisciplinarity</em>. Since then, many books have been written, articles published, and conferences held.  In the early 21st century, two prominent federal funding agencies (<a href="https://www.nsf.gov/od/oia/additional_resources/interdisciplinary_research/">NSF</a>, NIH) would articulate the importance of interdisciplinary research (and graduate education) through the <a href="http://www.nap.edu/catalog/11153/facilitating-interdisciplinary-research">publication </a>of <em>Facilitating Interdisciplinary Research</em> (2004, National Academies Press) and <em>NIH</em> <em><a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC1289386/">Roadmap</a> Interdisciplinary Research initiatives</em> (2005, National Institutes of Health).</p>
<p>With increased attention about interdisciplinary research including the notion of &#8220;<a href="https://www.whitehouse.gov/administration/eop/ostp/grand-challenges">grand challenges</a>&#8220;, the development and implementation of interdisciplinary programs followed.  Leading the discussions at the graduate level was the Council of Graduate Schools (CGS) that offered sessions during its annual meetings and summer workshops.  Having been a participant in these conversations, the focus was primarily on content knowledge and research methodologies cutting across disciplines and the development of graduate degree programs.  Examples of good practices exist (e.g.,<a href="http://www.gradschool.psu.edu/prospective-students/interdisciplinary-graduate-training/"> Penn State</a>, <a href="http://ucdavis.edu/research/interdisciplinary.html">University of California-Davis</a>, <a href="http://www.graduate.ucf.edu/InterdisciplinaryPrograms/">University of Central Florida</a>, <a href="https://www.grad.umn.edu/projects-priorities/interdisciplinaryinitiatives">University of Minnesota</a>, <a href="http://www.grad.washington.edu/students/interdisciplinary/">University of Washington</a>, <a href="http://interdisciplinary.graduateschool.vt.edu/home">Virginia Tech</a>).  At Virginia Tech, we have also established a <a href="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/vtige/">blog site</a> as a means of sharing research across interdisciplinary degree programs as well.  And all the while, very little discussion has occurred around interdisciplinary thinking and its relationship to graduate education until CGS President Suzanne Ortega invited Vice Provost Frances Leslie (UC Irvine) and me to facilitate such a discussion.  A great discussion occurred; the results of which will be shared in a different forum.  What follows here are my reflections and musings about interdisciplinary thinking in preparation for and after that session.</p>
<p>I have previously blogged about <a href="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/kpdtge/index.php/2012/03/28/interdisciplinary-thinking-pi-and-adaptive-innovators/">interdisciplinary thinking</a> and <a href="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/kpdtge/index.php/2014/10/27/i-t-and-pi-as-metaphors-for-graduate-education/">different metaphors</a> for graduate education.  I proposed the symbol of &#8220;<a href="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/kpdtge/index.php/2014/10/27/i-t-and-pi-as-metaphors-for-graduate-education/">pi</a>&#8221; as metaphor for interdisciplinary (transdisciplinary as well) <a href="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2012/03/images.jpg"><img data-attachment-id="65" data-permalink="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/kpdtge/index.php/2012/03/28/interdisciplinary-thinking-pi-and-adaptive-innovators/images/" data-orig-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2012/03/images.jpg" data-orig-size="225,225" data-comments-opened="1" data-image-meta="{&quot;aperture&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;credit&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;camera&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;caption&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;created_timestamp&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;copyright&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;focal_length&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;iso&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;shutter_speed&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;title&quot;:&quot;&quot;}" data-image-title="images" data-image-description="" data-medium-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2012/03/images.jpg" data-large-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2012/03/images.jpg" class="alignleft  wp-image-65" src="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2012/03/images-150x150.jpg" alt="images" width="36" height="36" srcset="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2012/03/images-150x150.jpg 150w , https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2012/03/images.jpg 225w " sizes="(max-width: 36px) 100vw, 36px" /></a>and preparing graduate students to become adaptive innovators.  My musings about interdisciplinary thinking continue and have been informed by Simeon Dreyfuss article entitled <a href="http://www.oakland.edu/upload/docs/AIS/Issues%20in%20Interdisciplinary%20Studies/2011%20Volume%2029/05_Vol_29_pp_67_83_Something_Essential_About_Interdisciplinary_Thinking_(Simeon_Dreyfuss).pdf">&#8220;Something essential about interdisciplinary thinking</a>&#8221; published in 2011 in <span style="text-decoration: underline">Issues in Integrative Studies</span> (29, 67-83)</p>
<p>So how do I understand interdisciplinary thinking?  Interdisciplinary thinking (I-thinking) must extend beyond the sharing of content and methodology from different disciplinary perspectives.  I-thinking must reach beyond common courses, shared research projects, case studies and joint publications. I-thinking most likely involves team science especially collaboration and clear, direct communication.</p>
<p>I-thinking should involve problem solving as well as problem defining and problem posing.  Yes, it involves what is known as critical thinking skills however these are defined. It is about asking questions and &#8220;sitting with&#8221; the question before jumping to solutions or answers quickly.  I-thinking takes time and requires perseverance.</p>
<p>Interdisciplinary thinking is about different ways of knowing and knowing differently and knowing in relationship to other even dissimilar views. It is about differing modalities of thinking and learning which requires acceptance of and tolerance for ambiguity and dissonance and perhaps confusion at times.  Creativity and innovation are key components and outcomes of interdisciplinary thinking.</p>
<p>Interdisciplinary thinking is a non-linear process and doesn&#8217;t embrace dualities but seeks intersections and connections.  I-thinking is about acknowledging the notion of a &#8220;baggy idea of truth, understanding the multiplicity of truth and the ongoing search for evolving truth.  It involves looking for and seeing the &#8220;<a href="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/kpdtge/index.php/2012/10/24/seeing-the-unobvious-fed-ex-and-other-visuals/">unobvious</a>&#8221; &#8211; to see things in ways which might not be obvious.</p>
<p>Interdisciplinary thinking is not only integrative but much more.  Beyond analysis and synthesis across disciplines, interdisciplinary thinking must be iterative and emergent.</p>
<p>Dreyfuss (2011) wrote that the difference between disciplinary and interdisciplinary thinking is &#8220;a manifestation of how deeply one is wed to particular historical institutionalizations of knowledge&#8221; (p. 80). In order to prepare the graduate students for the future, graduate deans must encourage programs and provide opportunities to push beyond the historical institutionalization of knowledge and disciplinary boundaries into interdisciplinary thinking.  The abilities and skills associated with interdisciplinary thinking will serve all graduate students well in discipline-based or interdisciplinary programs.</p>
<p>So, the question now is how.</p>


		<p>Academic bullying. In what ways does it manifest itself?  When does it occur? Why does it exist?  And most importantly, how can bullying be eliminated and an affirming environment for graduate education be enhanced?  The questions are many, the answers actually complex.</p>
<p>Academic bullying has become increasingly more visible in the past few years and might even to a<img data-attachment-id="1211" data-permalink="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/kpdtge/index.php/2015/10/18/academic-bullying/professors-behaving-badly/" data-orig-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2015/10/Professors-behaving-badly.jpg" data-orig-size="106,160" data-comments-opened="1" data-image-meta="{&quot;aperture&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;credit&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;camera&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;caption&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;created_timestamp&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;copyright&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;focal_length&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;iso&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;shutter_speed&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;title&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;orientation&quot;:&quot;0&quot;}" data-image-title="Professors behaving badly" data-image-description="" data-medium-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2015/10/Professors-behaving-badly.jpg" data-large-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2015/10/Professors-behaving-badly.jpg" class="alignleft size-thumbnail wp-image-1211" src="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2015/10/Professors-behaving-badly-106x150.jpg" alt="Professors behaving badly" width="106" height="150" />ppear as being more tolerated in higher education today.  One comprehensive study was conducted and the results published in 2011 by John M. Braxton, Eve Proper, and Alan E. Bayer entitled <em><a href="https://books.google.com/books?id=XoL_D5xxmJoC&amp;source=gbs_navlinks_s">Professors Behaving Badly: Faculty Misconduct in Graduate Education</a>.</em></p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<p>Several other books on bullying have been written in the last few years. Articles, reports and coverage within the higher education media (see articles in <a href="http://chronicle.com/">Chronicle of Higher Education</a>, <a href="https://www.insidehighered.com/">InsideHigherEd</a>) occur with some frequency.<img data-attachment-id="1209" data-permalink="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/kpdtge/index.php/2015/10/18/academic-bullying/faculty-incivility/" data-orig-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2015/10/faculty-incivility.jpg" data-orig-size="214,320" data-comments-opened="1" data-image-meta="{&quot;aperture&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;credit&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;camera&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;caption&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;created_timestamp&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;copyright&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;focal_length&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;iso&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;shutter_speed&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;title&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;orientation&quot;:&quot;0&quot;}" data-image-title="faculty incivility" data-image-description="" data-medium-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2015/10/faculty-incivility-201x300.jpg" data-image-title="workplace bullying in HE" data-image-description="" data-medium-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2015/10/workplace-bullying-in-HE-201x300.jpg" data-large-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2015/10/workplace-bullying-in-HE.jpg" class="alignleft size-thumbnail wp-image-1213" src="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2015/10/workplace-bullying-in-HE-150x150.jpg" alt="workplace bullying in HE" width="150" height="150" /> <img data-attachment-id="1212" data-permalink="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/kpdtge/index.php/2015/10/18/academic-bullying/bullying-in-ivory-tower/" data-orig-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2015/10/Bullying-in-Ivory-tower.jpg" data-orig-size="331,500" data-comments-opened="1" data-image-meta="{&quot;aperture&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;credit&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;camera&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;caption&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;created_timestamp&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;copyright&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;focal_length&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;iso&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;shutter_speed&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;title&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;orientation&quot;:&quot;0&quot;}" data-image-title="Bullying in Ivory tower" data-image-description="" data-medium-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2015/10/Bullying-in-Ivory-tower-199x300.jpg" data-large-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2015/10/Bullying-in-Ivory-tower.jpg" class="alignleft size-thumbnail wp-image-1212" src="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2015/10/Bullying-in-Ivory-tower-150x150.jpg" alt="Bullying in Ivory tower" width="150" height="150" /> Bullying exists in higher education and Graduate Schools must assume a leadership role in addressing the concerns for graduate education and graduate students.</p>
<p>Although the terminology of &#8216;academic bullying&#8217; is recent, the emphasis upon quality of and for graduate education extends back to at least the 1990s including prominent examples from AAU and the AAUP.  The Association of American Universities (AAU) established the <a href="https://www.aau.edu/policy/graded_background.aspx?id=6878">Committee on Graduate Education</a> and issued its report calling on universities to examine graduate education programs (<a href="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/kpdtge/files/2015/10/AAU-GradEd-report.pdf">AAU GradEd report).</a>  Shortly thereafter in 1999, the AAUP established policy about graduate education and <a href="http://www.aaup.org/issues/graduate-students/resources-graduate-students">resources </a>for graduate students.  Mentoring, collegial relationships and affirming education + equitable employment conditions were important and remain so. Civility should be the expectation and bullying should not be tolerated.</p>
<p>Today, most if not all of the U.S. Graduate Schools now have statements and documents in which they articulate the established principles for graduate education frequently referred to as principles or guiding principles, codes, or expectations. Even though the terminology of &#8216;academic bullying&#8217; might not be specifically mentioned, the intent of these documents is to articulate the expectations for quality graduate education and an affirming climate for graduate students.  This is the approach taken by the Virginia Tech Graduate School.  We entitled our document Expectations for Graduate Study in which the expectations for graduate students, faculty, departments/programs and the Graduate School were articulated.  A <a href="http://graduateschool.vt.edu/node/6042">website </a>was created along with a summary of the expectations <a href="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/kpdtge/files/2015/10/Expectation_Glance_2011.pdf">Expectation_Glance_2011</a>.</p>
<p>The VT Graduate School is committed to enhancing the graduate education experience and providing opportunities for graduate students to thrive.  We are changing the paradigm from survival, silence and acceptance of status quo to <a href="http://graduateschool.vt.edu/gs/tge">transforming graduate education</a>. The conversation about academic bullying will continue.  Watch for additional blog posts, information about models and promising practices, and ways to share concerns anonymously.</p>


		<p>The Virginia Tech Graduate School is committed to providing a &#8220;rich learning environment&#8221; for graduate students and a quality graduate education experience.  To do so requires clear communication about policy and procedures and especially the expectations for graduate study.  The VT Graduate School&#8217;s commitment is articulated in the &#8220;<a href="http://graduateschool.vt.edu/node/6042">Expectations for Graduate Education at Virginia Tech</a>.&#8221;</p>
<p>Originally created by graduate students and faculty in 2003, the expectations document was revised in 2011 and highlights the &#8220;vital elements of 21st-century graduate education, including ethics, civility, professional and academic development, teaching/learning, and inclusive community&#8221;.  The document describes the <a href="http://graduateschool.vt.edu/node/6051">graduate environment</a> promoted at VT and articulates expectations for <a href="http://graduateschool.vt.edu/node/6044">graduate students</a>, <a href="http://graduateschool.vt.edu/node/6045">faculty</a>, <a href="http://graduateschool.vt.edu/node/6046">departments</a> (programs, schools) and the <a href="http://graduateschool.vt.edu/node/6047">Graduate School</a>.  The document also includes a section on <a href="http://graduateschool.vt.edu/node/6059">mentoring</a> and a section on <a href="http://graduateschool.vt.edu/node/6060">complaints and the appeals process</a>.  A <a href="http://graduateschool.vt.edu/sites/default/files/u12/expectations/Expectation_Glance_2011.pdf">summary</a> of the expectations are shared in a printable format as well.</p>
<p>The expectations are discussed in relation to major components of graduate education: progress toward degree, research and ethics, teaching and training, professional development, assistantships and financial aid, and community.  Although written in 2011, the expectations still provide relevant guidelines for graduate education today. Examples include the following:</p>
<ul>
<li>Clear communication about departmental and Graduate School policy and procedures</li>
<li>Regular communication between faculty advisor and student</li>
<li>Understand and adhere to responsible conduct of research and scholarly endeavors</li>
<li>Discuss and agree upon requirements and expectation for authorship</li>
<li>Ensure that graduate programs conform to the highest academic standards and remain relevant through appropriate curriculum</li>
<li>Adherence to professional codes of conduct, student conduct and honor codes</li>
<li>Provide appropriate teacher training and professional development opportunities</li>
<li>Provide annual feedback to students and provide opportunity for students to correct academic deficiencies</li>
<li>Ensure fair and equitable treatment of students</li>
<li>Provide a safe and collegial work environment; respect work-life balance and working conditions</li>
<li>Provide pertinent resources and mentoring for degree completion</li>
</ul>
<div class="page" title="Page 1">While the information seems fairly straightforward and common sense, it is valuable to have the expectations clearly identified and shared with the graduate students and faculty in order to provide the foundation for a quality graduate education experience at the beginning of one&#8217;s graduate study and to remind faculty, departments and the Graduate School of our collective responsibility for quality graduate education. The goal of graduate education should be to provide the academic context(s) and mentoring that allows graduate students to demonstrate their ability to achieve, their desire to succeed and their commitment to future professional contributions.</div>
<div class="page" title="Page 1"></div>
<div class="page" title="Page 1">Unfortunately, academic bullying and questionable professional behavior are still prevalent in higher education.  And as I mentioned in the previous blog entitled <a href="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/kpdtge/index.php/2015/11/09/academic-bullying-and-graduate-education/">Academic Bullying and graduate education</a>, these must be eliminated from graduate education and replaced with a challenging yet affirming academic environment.</div>


		<p>A recent study by Gentry and Whitely (2014) entitled &#8220;Bullying in Graduate School: Its Nature and Effects&#8221; concluded that although graduate students did experience &#8216;aggressive and exclusionary&#8217; behaviors associated with traditional definition of bullying they tended to reject the use of &#8220;bullying&#8221; to describe their experiences.
