universities and cultural sites,interacting with university personnel (administrators, faculty, staff and students), sharing local food and beverages, and more.  It is about gaining knowledge and understanding and it is also about building relations and sharing time and space.  It is about professional and personal growth and development.</p>
<p>Based upon my experiences in leading the Global Perspectives Programs within the umbrella of the Transformative Graduate Education (<a href="http://graduateschool.vt.edu/transformative-graduate-education-experience/tge-initiative.html">TGE</a>) initiative,  I am convinced that it is possible to transcend and transform the typical graduate education experience in meaningful and significantly beneficial ways.  Graduate Deans can make a difference and should be engaged with the academic preparation and professional development of our graduate students as well as the personal growth and development important for life and work in the 21st century.</p>
<p>The experiences provided through the Global Perspectives programs for the past 12 years have allowed me to witness the development of strong connections between and among participants during each trip. (Note: the participants come from a variety of disciplines and usually do not know each other prior to the trip). Each GPP group tends to develop a special connection and bond which continues to evolve (visibly) across the days of the trip and exists well beyond.  And I am thankful for the opportunities to enhance my knowledge and learn something new every time.</p>
<p>This was the case for GPP&#8217;16 Ecuador.  We enjoyed our unique experience and shared some very special moments. As we came together from our different academic worlds and lived experiences, we spent time together, really listening and hearing each other, sharing stories and views and feeling &#8220;safe&#8221; enough to be brave in asking questions and engaging with difficult dialogues and challenging topics. Although not particularly articulated as outcomes, honesty and authenticity were anticipated and were realized.  The conversations were real.  There&#8217;s something to be said <a href="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2016/12/CyDlVa-WIAIAi8M.jpg"><img data-attachment-id="1664" data-permalink="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/kpdtge/index.php/2016/12/18/global-perspectives-program16-ecuador-gpp16-reflections-beyond/cydlva-wiaiai8m/" data-orig-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2016/12/CyDlVa-WIAIAi8M.jpg" data-orig-size="1024,768" data-comments-opened="1" data-image-meta="{&quot;aperture&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;credit&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;camera&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;caption&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;created_timestamp&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;copyright&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;focal_length&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;iso&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;shutter_speed&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;title&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;orientation&quot;:&quot;0&quot;}" data-image-title="CyDlVa-WIAIAi8M" data-image-description="" data-medium-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2016/12/CyDlVa-WIAIAi8M-300x225.jpg" data-large-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2016/12/CyDlVa-WIAIAi8M-1024x768.jpg" class="alignleft size-thumbnail wp-image-1664" src="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2016/12/CyDlVa-WIAIAi8M-150x150.jpg" alt="" width="150" height="150" /></a>about getting away from our daily (and typical) environments to sitting on a large balcony with a view of the ocean to stimulate conversation and communication. It is important to note that communication and connection occur not just as a result of conversation but occur in other ways. We were comfortable with silence as important in sharing time and space and connection. This become evident during our Ecuador experience. The connections were and are real.</p>
<p>Given the strength of our connection, we were able to engage in authentic discussions of serious topics which continued throughout the trip.  At one point, I was asked to describe the &#8220;whys&#8221; of the decision-making process for the GPP experiences. The question led me to reflect upon the intentionality of the process and the principles by which the experience evolves. There is reasoned and reasonable intentionality behind the logistics, sequence, the visits, expectations and anticipated outcomes. As a result of the question and conversation that followed, I have continued to examine the underlying philosophical underpinnings and the principles for the program. This is the easy part for these can be described. The more challenging part of the answer rests in the process of decision-making which unfolds organically, and mostly goes unnoticed, throughout the trip and lies at the essence of the GPP experience. It is this essence and genuineness that creates the long-lasting connections among the GPP participants.</p>
<p>Shortly after our return from GPP16 Ecuador, the Fall semester came to a close with graduate commencement ceremonies. This year like previous years, there were several graduates who have participated in the Global Perspectives Program (GPP) and are now not <a href="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2016/12/1465848464308.jpg"><img data-attachment-id="1670" data-permalink="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/kpdtge/index.php/2016/12/18/global-perspectives-program16-ecuador-gpp16-reflections-beyond/attachment/1465848464308/" data-orig-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2016/12/1465848464308.jpg" data-orig-size="1024,695" data-comments-opened="1" data-image-meta="{&quot;aperture&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;credit&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;camera&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;caption&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;created_timestamp&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;copyright&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;focal_length&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;iso&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;shutter_speed&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;title&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;orientation&quot;:&quot;0&quot;}" data-image-title="1465848464308" data-image-description="" data-medium-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2016/12/1465848464308-300x204.jpg" data-large-file="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2016/12/1465848464308-1024x695.jpg" class="alignright size-thumbnail wp-image-1670" src="https://gqcyy0ylcr0jgvhx-zippykid.netdna-ssl.com/kpdtge/files/2016/12/1465848464308-150x150.jpg" alt="" width="150" height="150" /></a>only VT alumni but also GPP alumni.  As each one crossed the stage to receive their degree, we shared a moment in which the special connection of the GPP experience was present and very real.</p>


		<p>Fifty years ago, Robert F. Kennedy introduced the phrase that &#8220;one may live in interesting times&#8221;.  He stated that &#8220;l<span class="_Tgc">ike it or not, <b>we live in interesting times</b>. They are <b>times</b> of danger and uncertainty; but they are also the most creative of any time in the history&#8230;&#8221; (speech given in Cape Town, June 1966).  It appears to me that today, we are also living in &#8216;interesting times&#8217;.  For most, uncertainty and danger for many are clearly perceived for 2017 and beyond. There is so much that is unknown at the moment that it becomes unsettling. But perhaps these post-2016 election times might also challenge us, the words of RFK to be &#8220;creative&#8221;. That is, to ponder, reflect and act.<br />
</span></p>
<p><span class="_Tgc">In her book entitled <em>Hope in the Dark</em>, Rebecca Solnit (2015 reissue of 2004 book) writes  about &#8216;hope&#8217; but not as optimism per se but rather that &#8220;hope locates itself in the premises that we d</span><img class="alignleft" src="https://haymarket-prod.s3.amazonaws.com/images/000000/843/9781608465767-f_medium-2f1e8dbafb4b3334d0db297eed405179.jpg" alt="9781608465767-f_medium" width="125" height="184" /><span class="_Tgc">on’t know what will happen and that in the spaciousness of uncertainty is room to act. When you recognize uncertainty, you recognize that you may be able to influence the outcomes.” (from her <a href="https://www.facebook.com/rebecca.solnit/posts/10154521270090552">Facebook </a>page in November 2016).    </span>&#8220;Optimists think it will all be fine without our involvement; pessimists take the opposite position; both excuse themselves from acting. Hope is an embrace of the unknown and the unknowable, an alternative to the certainty of both optimists and pessimists. It’s the belief that what we do matters even though how and when it may matter, who and what it may impact, are not things you can know beforehand.&#8221;   As she proposes, recognizing uncertainty allows us to recognize that we might be &#8220;able to influence the outcomes&#8221;.  Thus, it appears that now is the time to take action.</p>
<p>Although a rather simplistic statement, the 2016 elections revealed so much more about the current state of U.S. society and higher education&#8217;s connection (or lack thereof) to that reality for many. Higher education has been often accused of being elitist and out of touch with society and I would argue that sometimes we have been. University towns are sometimes called a &#8216;blue bubble&#8217; in an otherwise red state. A recent article from the Chronicle of Higher Education highlighted the phenomena of &#8220;<a href="http://www.chronicle.com/article/Blue-Bubble-in-a-Red-State/238896?cid=wb&amp;utm_source=wb&amp;utm_medium=en&amp;elqTrackId=d7df41397f8e4f86be16834374718269&amp;elq=b0f6af0cd08f43ed820ccca0eccc1982&amp;elqaid=12159&amp;elqat=1&amp;elqCampaignId=4915">blue bubbles</a>&#8221;  and provided some perspective on why universities are sometimes isolated from the surrounding communities. And this is where change must begin.</p>
<p>From the perspective of higher education, I would argue (along with many others) that higher education has not only a role to play but a responsibility to get involved and even to assume a leadership role.  As educational institutions, colleges and universities must continue to educate our students as well as our faculty, staff and administrators about social justice, equity and civil discourse.  We must be intentional about engaging with the dialogue around difference, encouraging all to speak up and speak out and to do so by understanding difference and through listening and hearing the voices of others.  It is also important that we focus our attention to communicate with clarity and to enhance our skills and ability to determine the accuracy of information and seek truth.  Articles about programs, strategies and workshops as well as analyses, opinion pieces and reflections are found frequently in publications including the <em>Chronicle of Higher Education</em>, <em>InsideHigherEd, </em>and<em> Times Higher Education</em> to name only a few.</p>
<p>Let me offer a few examples.</p>
<p>In the days following the 2016 election, an increase in <a href="http://www.chronicle.com/blogs/ticker/heres-a-rundown-of-the-latest-campus-climate-incidents-since-trumps-election/115553">hate motivated campus-climate incidents</a> occurred and was reported by the Chronicle of Higher Education.  The data were compiled by Southern Poverty Law Center which issued a <a href="https://www.splcenter.org/20161129/ten-days-after-harassment-and-intimidation-aftermath-election">report </a>including historical context and detailed information about type and location of the hate-related incidents.  I believe that in part these data provided the impetus for the call for higher education to respond and a focus on citizenship was one such response. Although there are many others, recent articles suggest <a href="http://www.chronicle.com/article/How-Colleges-Can-Teach/238891?cid=gn&amp;utm_source=gn&amp;utm_medium=en&amp;elqTrackId=f184d42584d74ba596cc161d6cfe3998&amp;elq=0f4a11a9cb3e4e5e9d2afa47a617c500&amp;elqaid=12207&amp;elqat=1&amp;elqCampaignId=4945">how colleges can teach students to be good citizens</a> and urge colleges and universities <a href="http://www.chronicle.com/article/Don-t-Retreat-Teach/238923?cid=at&amp;utm_source=at&amp;utm_medium=en&amp;elqTrackId=24e030f1eaf944f6b01de5f18f24e176&amp;elq=0e3b85599df44e9abae4cd60c6d6e61d&amp;elqaid=12208&amp;elqat=1&amp;elqCampaignId=4946">not to retreat but rather to teach citizenship</a>.  Examples of programs and initiatives for understanding difference, increasing awareness of micro-aggressions and implicit bias and sustaining affirming campus-climate environment appear regularly in the higher education news and social media.</p>
<p>Given the rhetoric of the 2016 election campaign, it has become very clear that &#8220;racism still exists and can appear&#8221; on university campuses according to racial-equity scholar <a href="http://www.chronicle.com/article/A-Racial-Equity-Scholar/238843?cid=at&amp;utm_source=at&amp;utm_medium=en&amp;elqTrackId=ce21384a176045a995b99f9275a186ac&amp;elq=377562364dfb414ea6060ed342e69576&amp;elqaid=12042&amp;elqat=1&amp;elqCampaignId=4857">Harper </a>(2017) in a recent <a href="http://www.chronicle.com/article/A-Racial-Equity-Scholar/238843?cid=at&amp;utm_source=at&amp;utm_medium=en&amp;elqTrackId=ce21384a176045a995b99f9275a186ac&amp;elq=377562364dfb414ea6060ed342e69576&amp;elqaid=12042&amp;elqat=1&amp;elqCampaignId=4857">Chronicle of Higher Education article</a>.  &#8220;The polarizing nature of the 2016 campaign makes improving the racial climate a more urgent matter for higher-education leaders.&#8221;  Once again,this speaks to the opportunity and the need to act and educate.</p>
<p>Education is critical and universities must do their part.  Universities can provide opportunity, programs, space (real and metaphorical) for dialogue, and messages that foster inclusion.   A recent example of a timely message is the address provided by <a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=u51wvuEL3-c&amp;feature=youtu.be">Andy Morikawa</a> (Blacksburg, Virginia) at the December 2016 Virginia Tech <a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=u51wvuEL3-c&amp;feature=youtu.be">Graduate School Commencement</a>.  (Note: his remarks begin at minute 35 on the recording).  Morikawa encouraged us to get involved, get engaged in civic life and community engagement, to be attentive, to listen, to have tough conversations with those who don&#8217;t share the same views and to do so regularly in community.</p>
<p>As we know education is a primary mission of higher education and for many universities, research is also a primary mission.  Science, discovery and the search for truth are critical and remain even more so in the post 2016 election era.  Besides &#8216;teaching citizenship&#8217; and encouraging civil discourse, how do we engage our students with determining facts and uncovering &#8216;fake news&#8217;?  A recent article from Times Higher Education (THE) suggests that it is <a href="https://www.timeshighereducation.com/blog/fake-news-solution-education-not-regulation">education not regulation</a>.  Seargeant and Tagg (2016)  wrote that &#8220;the heightened need for critical literacy skills in tackling fake news and media manipulation highlights the central role that higher education can play for society as a whole.&#8221;  Further, Virgo (2017) writing in Times Higher education suggests that the university must accept its “<a href="https://www.timeshighereducation.com/comment/university-critic-and-conscience-society">role as critic and conscience of society</a>”.</p>
<p>In this post-2016 election era, faculty and academic administrators have much to contemplate not only about our defined missions in research, teaching, and engagement but also as critic and conscience of society in accepting the responsibility of the university as a social institution and to do so with &#8220;<a href="https://blogs.lt.vt.edu/kpdtge/index.php/2016/10/28/intentional-and-ethical-scholar-activism/">intentional and ethical scholar activism</a>&#8220;.</p>
<p>Higher education has the responsibility to be &#8216;creative&#8217; and innovative in these &#8216;interesting times&#8217; and to embrace the unknown and act so we can &#8216;influence the outcomes&#8217;.  Let us work individually as well as collectively.</p>
